DTU Wind and Energy Systems is a department of the 
Technical University of Denmark with a unique 
integration of research, education, innovation and 
public/private sector consulting in the field of wind and 
energy. Our activities develop new opportunities and 
technology for the global and Danish exploitation of 
wind and energy. Research focuses on key technical-
scientific fields, which are central for the development, 
innovation and use of wind energy and provides the 
basis for advanced education.
Title:
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based 
offshore wind farms
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The integration of wind energy into the power grid is of utmost importance in meeting the
growing demand for renewable energy sources. High Voltage Direct Current (HVDC)
transmission systems have emerged as a promising solution for offshore wind farms,
offering advantages over traditional AC transmission systems.
However, analysing
transient events for protection of such systems, particularly short circuit faults, poses
challenges in terms of simulation complexity. This master’s thesis aims to address these
challenges by proposing a simplified method for calculating short circuit currents in
PowerFactory, based on the IEC 60909 standard, and comparing its accuracy against a
detailed Electromagnetic Transient (EMT) model implemented in PSCAD.
The study commences with an extensive review of literature encompassing wind
turbines, HVDC transmission systems, mathematical tools employed for fault analysis as
well as a description of the International Electrotechnical Commission (IEC) 60909
standard fundamentals. Subsequently, a comprehensive standalone model of the wind
turbines and the HVDC transmission system is developed in PSCAD and their behaviour
during faults is characterised.
Later,
two analogous models are obtained in
PowerFactory and a comparative assessment under steady state conditions is
conducted to evaluate the accuracy and reliability of the PowerFactory standalone
equivalent against the detailed EMT model in PSCAD.
Upon validating the standalone models, two symmetrical models of an HVDC-based
offshore wind farm are constructed in both PowerFactory and PSCAD. Multiple fault
scenarios are simulated at various locations within the wind farm, capturing the
contributions of selected components to the fault currents in both software programs.
The outcomes of this thesis encompass the comparative analysis of short circuit
currents between the IEC 60909-based model in PowerFactory and the one in PSCAD,
contributing to the field of electrical engineering by providing a simplified method for
short circuit analysis and ultimately assessing the suitability of the IEC 60909 for fault
analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms.
Keywords: Offshore wind farm, HVDC transmission, transient fault analysis, Modular
Multilevel Converters (MMCs), Electromagnetic Transient (EMT) model, PowerFactory,
PSCAD.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms

Approval
This thesis has been prepared over six months at the Wind Energy Systems Division,
Department of Wind and Energy Systems, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU,
in partial fulfilment for the degree Master of Science (MSc) in Electrical Engineering, MSc
Eng.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge of power systems.

==PAGE:16==
Part I
Introduction
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
1
==PAGE:17==
1
Introduction
The integration of wind energy into the power grid has become crucial in the global
pursuit of sustainable and renewable energy sources.
In recent times, the energy
transition in the context of the European Green Deal has been accelerated, increasing
the share of renewable energy targets from 32% to 42.5% [1]. As the demand for clean
energy continues to grow, offshore wind farms have gained significant attention due to
their potential to harness strong and consistent wind resources. To efficiently transmit
the generated power from these offshore wind farms to the onshore grid, HVDC
transmission systems have emerged as a promising solution, offering advantages over
traditional High Voltage Alternating Current (HVAC) transmission systems, such as
lower transmission losses and improved controllability.
However, successful integration of offshore wind farms with HVDC transmission
systems requires robust protection mechanisms to ensure safe and reliable operation.
Among various transient events, short circuit faults pose significant challenges to the
protection of these systems.
Analysing transient behaviour accurately during short
circuit faults is crucial for designing effective protection strategies, but it often involves
complex simulations due to the intricacies of the system components and their
interactions.
This master’s thesis aims to simplify the analysis of short circuit faults in HVDC-based
offshore wind farms.
This is performed by (1) proposing a simplified method for
calculating short circuit currents in such installations based on the IEC 60909 standard
and (2) benchmarking the accuracy of such method against a detailed Electromagnetic
Transient (EMT) model implemented in PSCAD.
Research begins with an extensive review of the existing literature, encompassing wind
turbines, HVDC transmission systems, and the mathematical tools used for transient
analysis. In the methodology part, the proposed method for short circuit current analysis
is first presented. Then, a comprehensive standalone model of the wind turbine and the
HVDC transmission system is developed in PSCAD to characterise their behaviour
during short circuit faults. Afterwards, analogous models of wind turbines and the HVDC
transmission system are obtained in PF. A comparative evaluation is then performed to
evaluate the precision and reliability of these standalone PF models against the detailed
ones implemented in PSCAD. Once the individual models have been validated, a model
of an offshore wind farm is constructed in both PF and PSCAD. Multiple fault scenarios
are simulated at various locations within the wind farm, measuring the contribution to the
fault current of the offshore Modular Multilevel Converter and selected wind turbines.
Furthermore, the accuracy of the method is discussed by comparison with the
benchmark implemented in PSCAD.
The results of this thesis contribute to the field of electrical engineering by providing a
comparative analysis of short circuit currents between PF and PSCAD and, ultimately, by
assessing the suitability of the IEC 60909 standard for fault analysis in offshore wind farms
with HVDC transmission. Furthermore, the proposed simplified method helps to develop
effective protection strategies, thereby promoting the reliable and secure operation of
these systems. By simplifying the analysis of transient short circuit events, this research
contributes to the larger goal of achieving a sustainable energy future.
2
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:18==
2
Scope of the study
As stated above, the objectives of the present work are as follows.
• To propose a method to simplify the obtention of short circuit currents in HVDC-
based wind farms under the IEC 60909 standard.
• To analyse the precision of this method, based on the IEC 60909 standard, by
benchmarking against a detailed version of the same wind farm in a EMT-based
software.
So as to investigate the behaviour of faults in such type of installations, a wind farm model
featuring an HVDC link was developed. This connection to the shore is made up of a pair
of MMCs with a large cable. In addition, three different strings, both in layout and distance,
simulate possible topologies in Wind farm (WF) layouts. Each of the strings has seven 12
MVA wind turbines connected and is fed to the MMC by a 300 MVA transformer. Naturally,
several assumptions were made during the investigation.
• The three strings mentioned above only cover one third of the HVDC link capacity.
It is presumed that in reality, the remaining two-thirds would be connected to the
MMC through the corresponding 300 MVA transformers.
• Voltage dip occurs when the bus voltages fall below 0.9 p.u.
the nominal one,
according to the Danish grid code [2].
• In PSCAD, the VSC are modelled with an average model employing controllable
voltage sources instead of power electronics. This approximation is taken with a
view to reduce simulating time.
• The MMC and the wind turbines control and limit only the positive sequence current.
The negative sequence impedance of these is approximated by a tuning method.
• These current controls cause the fault current waveform obtained from PSCAD to
differ largely from those defined in the standard.
As a result, the characteristic
parameters of the fault current have been estimated as the RMS value of the
instant current values, which introduces a small error.
• Wind turbines in PF are modelled as wind power stations, according to the IEC
60909. This implies that the zero-sequence impedance of the wind turbine is infinite.
• The fault current contribution from the MMC and WT has been estimated to be the
same during the maximum and minimum fault current events defined in the standard.
• The tuning method pursues the adjustment of the sequence currents magnitude,
but does not deal with the phase angle.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms

==PAGE:19==
Part II
Theoretical background
4
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:20==
In recent times, the use of wind turbines for power generation has gained significant
momentum and, consequently, allocated room for novel technologies. This part of the
master thesis addresses the fundamentals of the main equipment and tools used
throughout this project.
Initially, an in-depth exploration of wind turbines is provided, introducing their various
types and fundamental operating principles. Furthermore, the role of generators and the
conversion of wind power into usable electrical energy through VSC is discussed.
Secondly, the focus shifts to HVDC links, which have recently become increasingly
popular in the field of power transmission.
Specifically, the structure, operation, and
control modes of Modular Multilevel Converters (MMC) are investigated as key
components within HVDC systems.
Lastly, this section explores the theoretical background for short-circuit analysis, starting
with a short introduction to symmetrical components.
Following on, the IEC 60909
standard for high voltage short circuit analysis is presented, with a view to the
assumptions in wind power units with full-size converters.
Altogether, the present part aims to (1) establish the theoretical foundation of this master
thesis and (2) facilitate the comprehension of the models presented in the following
chapters.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
5
==PAGE:21==
3
The basics of wind turbines
A wind turbine can be described as a mechanical device designed to capture the kinetic
energy of the wind and convert it into rotational mechanical energy, which is then used to
generate electrical power through the operation of an attached generator. Wind turbines
can be classified after criteria such as the type of generator or the degree to which the
speed can be controlled.
3.1
Types of wind turbines
According to [3], there are two main types of wind turbines, depending on whether they
operate at fixed or variable speeds.
Fixed-speed type
These were the first wind turbines to be released and employ Squirrel Cage Induction
Generator (SCIG), which normally operate at low speeds. These are directly tied to the
grid and, due to the induction machine characteristics, high inrush currents are expected.
To compensate for these, soft starters such as the one in fig. 3.1 are normally used to
reduce the winding current [4]. Although this type of wind turbine is more robust and
affordable than the newer types due to the smaller number of components, its efficiency
is limited by the fact that maximum power extraction is carried out at a single wind speed,
which is why we name this type single-speed wind turbines and more commonly known
as Type I wind turbines.
Figure 3.1: (Type I) Single-speed wind turbine (from [4]).
A more flexible configuration of the fixed speed in terms of operating conditions is obtained
by having a variable number of poles in the SCIG or having two generators in parallel, one
for high speed and the other for low speed. These two types are named Two-speed wind
turbines
Variable-speed types
Variable-speed wind turbines refer to those that can reach maximum power extraction at
different wind speeds. There are three main configurations (types II, III and IV) that can
accomplish this.
The second type is known as Variable-slip wind turbine, and generally has a Wound-rotor
Induction Generator (WRIG) as in fig. 3.2. This type of generator has the ability to vary
its torque-slip characteristics with rotor resistance. When an external rotor resistance is
introduced, a variation of speed can be reached in the range of 10% the nominal one,
thus offering greater flexibility than the single speed type. This model is relatively simple
compared to types III and IV, although it presents resistive losses due to the external rotor
resistor.
6
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:22==
Figure 3.2: (Type II) Variable slip wind turbine (from [4]).
Type III is one of the most used configurations today and features a Doubly-Fed Induction
Generator (DFIG) as in fig. 3.3. Here, the need for reactive power compensation and
a soft starter disappears and the external rotor resistor is replaced by a limited-capacity
power converter [4]). This converter controls the power factor and is sized only to account
for the slip control, so it is more affordable than the full-capacity equivalent converters.
Additionally, the use of converters allows power to flow in both directions, increasing the
operating range of the wind turbine.
Figure 3.3: (Type III) Reduced-capability converter wind turbine (from [4]).
The fourth type of wind turbine extends the power converter capacity and allows the
generator to fully decouple from the grid as in fig. 3.4 [4].
The rating power of the
converter is usually as high as the generator rating, which increases the cost. However,
reactive compensation can be performed, and a smoother connection to the grid is
obtained. Additionally, this topology allows greater flexibility in terms of generator type,
as
it
can
pair
with
SCIG,
WRIG,
and
Permanent
Magnet
Synchronous
Generators (PMSG). The latter is a special case where the gearbox can be omitted if a
large number of poles is used.
Naturally, this further reduces maintenance cost,
although larger generators are required.
Figure 3.4: (Type IV) Full-capability converter wind turbine (from [4]).
Nowadays, a vast majority of the HVDC-based transmission offshore wind farms are
based in Type 4 wind turbines due to the more flexible operation at variable wind speeds
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
7
==PAGE:23==
and it is assumed that this will remain true in the foreseeable future.
3.2
The aerodynamic model of a wind turbine
In order to maximise the power extraction from wind gusts, it is crucial to understand the
aerodynamic phenomena involved. Figure 3.5 presents a general view of a wind turbine
and the main characteristics of aerodynamics.
Figure 3.5: View of the main aerodynamic parameters in a wind turbine (from [5]).
In fig. 3.5, ⃗
vtip is the linear speed of the tip of the rotating blade in m/s. This is obtained
from the angular speed of the shaft ωwtr in rad/s and the radius R in metres as:
⃗
vtip = ωwtr · R
(3.1)
Moreover, λ is the tip speed ratio, linking the blade tip speed ⃗
vtip with the wind speed ⃗U:
λ = ⃗
vtip
⃗U
(3.2)
The relative speed of the wind ⃗W is found by subtracting the tip speed ⃗
vtip from the wind
speed ⃗U. The pitch angle θ is obtained as the angle between the chord line and the tip
speed as shown in fig. 3.5. Additionally, the angle of attack α is defined as the angle
between the relative speed of the wind ⃗W and the chord line. Note that the size of this
angle depends on the pitch angle θ, the wind speed ⃗U and the tip speed ⃗
vtip [5]).
By decomposing the wind force into its two components and taking the relative wind speed
⃗W as a reference, two forces are found:
• The lift force (L), which is 90°apart from the direction of the relative speed of the
wind.
• The drag force (D) that acts in the direction of the relative speed of the wind.
It should be noted that optimal power extraction is achieved when the lift-to-drag ratio is
maximum. The power coefficient Cp is defined as the relationship between the power that
is extracted and the power that is available from the wind gusts [5]. This parameter has a
maximum value of 0.59 and depends on wind speed ⃗U, rotor speed ωwtr, and pitch angle
θ, but is normally given as a function of λ and θ.
The aerodynamic control in the wind turbine has the task of adapting the pitch and the
rotor speed to meet the maximum power coefficient Cp−max, given for optimal λopt and
8
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:24==
θopt. Normally, wind turbine manufacturers provide a Cp−max profile as the one displayed
in fig. 3.6. This is later implemented as a lookup table with λ and θ as inputs and Cp as
output.
Figure 3.6: Example of an MPPT plot (from [6]).
Finally, the aerodynamic power can be calculated for steady state conditions using
eq. (3.3).
Pwtr = 1
2ρAU3Cp(λ, θ)
(3.3)
As expected, active power will vary with wind speed. Thus, three areas can be defined
given the wind speed and the power output of the wind turbine as shown in fig. 3.7 [7].
• Parking region: This region is delimited by cut-in and cut-out speeds that are too
low and too large to operate. Typical values for these wind speeds are 3 and 25
m/s, respectively. The angle of the blade is maximum to block the movement of the
rotor.
• MPPT region: the aerodynamic model sets the pitch angle θ to its optimal value and
the tip speed ratio λ is controlled to extract as much power from the wind gusts as
possible. Note that even with the controllers working properly, the power output is
well below the rated value as there is not enough wind speed.
• Pitch control region: the aerodynamic model controls the pitch angle θ to keep the
active output power at a constant rated value.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
9
==PAGE:25==
Figure 3.7: Operating regions of a typical wind turbine (from [7]).
3.3
Generators in wind turbines
In the field of wind turbines, those components that transform wind mechanical power
into electrical power are known as generators.
These are mainly classified as
synchronous and asynchronous generators, which differ in the relation between the rotor
and the electrical angular speed. The latter, ωe, is determined by the frequency of the
grid f to which the generator is connected [8].
ωe = 2π · f
(3.4)
The synchronous speed ωgen also depends on the number of pair of poles N:
ωgen = 2π · f
N
(3.5)
The synchronous speed ωgen is equal to the angular speed of the rotor ωm for synchronous
generators. In an asynchronous machine, this is not true, since the angular speed of the
rotor relies on an extra parameter called slip s [8]. In the latter type of generator, the
synchronous speed ωgen and the rotor angular speed ωm are linked by:
s = ωgen −ωm
ωgen
(3.6)
As deducted in eq. (3.5), an increase in the number of poles reduces the rotor speed.
This fact is used by direct drive wind turbines, which omit the gearbox and handle the
lower rotating speed of the shaft by increasing the number of poles in the synchronous
generator [8]. As an example, gear-less PMSG enable a higher overall efficiency due
to lower maintenance requirements. However, these do come with a larger weight of
magnets and require the use of a power electronics interface [9].
3.4
Power conversion in wind turbines
To connect the wind turbine, which operates at variable speeds, to a fixed frequency
network, a power converter is needed. In fig. 3.8, a first power converter transforms the
AC voltage from the machine side into the DC voltage. A capacitor ensures that the DC
voltage is relatively constant and the chopper limits temporary overvoltages by dissipating
10
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:26==
energy through a resistor. Finally, a grid-side converter transforms this DC voltage back
into AC voltage.
Figure 3.8: Example of a DC link employed in wind turbines (from [10]).
3.4.1
Types of converters
These converters are traditionally classified by the power electronic they take as a base.
Line Commutated Converters
The first type are the Line-Commutated Converter (LCC), which use thyristors. Only the
turn-on instants of these can be controlled —the turn-off sequence is determined by line
commutation. The switchoff is produced when the voltage in the terminals is reversed,
thus creating a stair-shaped output current waveform. This output current has a high
contribution of low-frequency harmonics [11], which need to be dampened by large AC
filters.
On the DC side, large inductors keep the DC current almost constant. This limits the
current flow to one direction. However, thyristors have a higher current capacity than
Insulated Gate Bipolar Transistors (IGBT) and lower power losses.
Voltage Source Converters
The VSC, employ IGBT, in which both the instantaneous turn-on and the instantaneous
turn-off can be controlled by Pulse Width Modulation. VSC-based DC links employ a
capacitor in parallel, ensuring that the DC voltage is kept more or less constant.
The DC current is, in this case, not constant. Although it has a lower current carrying
capacity than LCC, limited by IGBT characteristics, the current in the DC link can flow in
both directions, allowing for an almost instant power reversal. Furthermore, the use of
PWM allows for the obtention of quasisinusoidal currents on the AC side. Line inductors
can be used to further smooth these currents [11].
While still most HVDC projects employ LCC, there has been a growing trend towards
pulse-controlled inverters based on IGBT, with a penetration of 40% in the market between
2016 and 2020 [12]. In [13] and [14], a three-phase, two level VSC is introduced, which
is shown in fig. 3.9.
The VSC can be modelled by eq. (3.7), eq. (3.8) and eq. (3.9) if a three-phase balanced
system with no neutral path is assumed [13]. Furthermore, the resistance ron in the IGBT
is neglected.
C dudc

3.4.2
The DQ reference frame
Equation (3.7), eq. (3.8) and eq. (3.9) refer to an ABC three-phase reference frame as
seen in fig. 3.10. The ABC reference frame axes are 2π/3 radians apart in space. The
magnitude value on each axis can be determined by projecting the vector ⃗x on the different
axes, obtaining the coordinates [xa, xb, xc]. Note that, in this section, subscripts a, b, c refer
to 1, 2, 3 from the previous section.
In case of a balanced system, xa + xb + xc = 0, so only two axes are necessary to
describe ⃗x completely. A so-called DQ frame can be defined by two axes, separated by
π/2 radians, and rotating with angular speed ωf.
To obtain the DQ frame coordinates from those expressed in the ABC reference frame,
the transformation in eq. (3.10) is used [4]. Note that θ is the angle between the A and D
axes.

3.4.3
Control modes of a VSC
Converters are typically configured to control either DC voltage or active power on the
D axis and AC voltage or reactive power on the Q axis. In the case of the wind turbine
converters, these are normally configured as follows:
• Grid-side converter
14
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:30==
– D axis: DC voltage control
– Q axis: grid-side AC voltage control
• Machine-side converter
– D axis: P control
– Q axis: machine-side AC voltage control
These converter control modes are implemented as grid-following, which means that none
of the converters is setting any frequency. Therefore, this frequency is imposed by the
external grid or third converters such as the one present in islanding HVDC-link MMC.
Note that, for the scope of the thesis, a more detailed explanation is given only for the
grid-side converter.
In eq. (3.10) it is seen that, to calculate the DQ transformation of grid voltages e123 and
currents i123, the angle θ between the A and D axes is needed. This is normally computed
with a Phase-Locked Loop (PLL) as the one shown in fig. 3.13. Note that the reference
voltage eq∗of the reactive Q-axis is set to zero.

This decomposes the voltage of the grid e123 = [e1, e2, e3] from fig. 3.9 into its ed and

between the measured value eq and the reference eq∗= 0. The output of this PI is
integrated and the resulting angle is wrapped between 0 and 2π radians. This angle θ is
then fed back to the ABC/DQ transformation block and has a sawtooth waveform. Note
that this component does not play any role in controlling the grid voltage, it just reads the
grid frequency.
A proportional integrator is a type of controller described by eq. (3.17).
Kp is the
proportional gain and τi is the integral time constant. Alternatively, an integral gain is
defined as Ki = Kp
τi . This type of control performs well, reducing the steady state error
to zero when the controlled signal has a DC waveform. This is the main advantage of
employing the DQ transformation: it allows one to represent sinusoidal waveforms as
DC signals and simplifies their control by using PI.
G(s) = Kp + Kp
τi(s)
(3.17)
When angle θ is known, the DQ transformations are computed for grid voltage e123 and
the current of the grid i123 = [i1, i2, i3], which deliver ed, eq, id and iq, respectively. For
the converter modes mentioned above, the active power reference P∗is obtained either
from the wind park controller or as an output of the aerodynamic model. Regarding the
reactive axis control, the park controller will provide either the reference voltage Vac∗or
the reactive power Q∗.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms

acts, reducing this error until it turns null in steady state. The output of the PI is the active
D id∗or reactive Q iq∗axis reference current, which is then fed to the current control.

One crucial consideration to be made regarding the control modes is the speed of the
different control loops involved. In the proposed setup, one faster, inner current control
loop is used. Reference to this inner control loop is generated by outer control loops. Note
that the innermost loop is the one involving the IGBT, which is done at a frequency of 1
up to 20 kHz. In [15], the bandwidth of the inner current loop PI is chosen between 1/20
and 1/10 of the switching frequency. Then, the outer voltage loop PI bandwidth is limited
to be between 1/50 and 1/10 of the inner loop one. As a result, (1) the outer loop control
target can be considered constant during inner loop tuning due to its slower response,
and (2) the inner current loop can be simplified as a unit proportion when tuning the outer
voltage loop.
Pulse Width Modulation
Firing pulses for IGBT S1-S6 in fig. 3.9 are calculated using the pulse width modulation
(PWM) technique, which takes three reference modulating waveforms Va, Vb, and Vc and
compares them with the carrier wave Vcr. The amplitude modulation index is defined as
the relationship between the peak values of both waves [4]:
ma =

voltages fluctuate between -VDC and +VDC.
When the frequency modulation index is an integer, the carrier and modulating waveforms
are synchronised. In two-level converters, the switching frequency fsw also matches that
of the carrier fcr [4]. Note the fundamental component Vab1 in fig. 3.18, whose magnitude
and frequency are controlled by the indices ma and mf.

Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:34==
4
Introduction to Modular Multilevel
Converters
Once the power is captured by the wind turbines and injected into the offshore wind farm
network, a connection to the onshore grid must be made. There are two main technologies
available to do this, namely HVAC or HVDC. Although traditionally AC transmission has
been chosen due to lower capital cost, recent improvements in the power electronics
segment are shifting the balance in favour of HVDC transmission for large power and
large distance to shore.
The advantages of HVDC transmission range from the absence of skin effect to lower
losses, especially in high-voltage, long-distance lines, due to the lack of reactive current
[16].
[16] suggests that the break-even distance —that length for which HVDC
transmission is more cost-effective over AC transmission— is currently around 50 km for
offshore transmission and points to a future decrease. However, this decision is, on a
practical level, conditioned not only by the transmission distance but also by more
factors, some of which may not be of technical nature.
HVDC transmission demands, however, the use of converters to switch between AC
and DC voltage. Different converter topologies can be employed —some of them were
previously seen, such as the LCC or the two-level VSC. Improvements in the scalability
and modularity of voltage-sourced converters led to the development of MMC [17],
which are later employed in this project.
4.1
The behaviour of an MMC
The basic unit of an MMC is the submodule, which is very similar to the configuration of
a VSC as that shown in fig. 3.9. By connecting submodules such as those displayed in
fig. 4.1a and fig. 4.1b in a series string, a higher voltage and number of voltage levels can
be achieved. If the net power exchange of one string over time is null and the submodules
are energetically balanced, it can be assumed that the submodules behave as controllable
voltage sources [18]. The idea behind the MMC is to use a large number of submodules
and consequently of levels, thus being able to replicate a sinusoidal waveform.
For the purpose of this report, a special focus will be put on the half-bridge submodule
in fig. 4.1a, which was first introduced in 2002 [18] and quickly became widespread. Its
performance is explained by two states which are exclusive to each other:
• Conduction (ON): the output voltage equals the submodule capacitor voltage uC
and there is a current flow in the capacitor. The switching function S is equal to one
when the submodule is in conducting state.
• Blocking (OFF): the output voltage is null and there is no current flowing in the
capacitor. The switching function S takes a null value.
In fig. 4.2 it is shown that MMC submodules are connected in a string. For each of the
phases, there is an upper (or top) and lower (or bottom) string, known as arm, with an
inductor L in series to prevent overcurrents during transients. The two arms of one phase
are called legs.
The first decision when modelling an MMC is to select the number of submodules n in each
of the arms, which will determine the number of levels as in levels = n + 1. Assuming the

4.2
Suppression of the circulating current
In eq. (4.7) and eq. (4.8), iza is the circulating current in phase A. A circulating current
is that current that, although not reflected on the AC side, flows between the arms and
therefore rises the overall RMS current, creating additional power losses. These losses
can be reduced if the circulating current is suppressed. This is addressed in [21], where
a common and differential mode circuit of the converter leg is studied, concluding that the
circulating current equals the differential current and also that this current waveform has
twice the fundamental frequency, thus being a harmonic of second order. This current
can be obtained from from eq. (4.7) and eq. (4.8):
iza = iupa + ilowa

In [21], the suppression control of the circulating current is presented in fig. 4.4. This is
based on the decomposition of circulating currents in its DQ components, compared by
two PI with the reference null currents.

differential voltages are found, which are then transformed back into the ABC reference

4.3
Balancing of capacitor voltages
In [19], assuming VDC = 4000V and five submodules, the capacitor voltage is known
from eq. (4.1) to be around uC = 800V . If the output AC voltage voa is now desired to
have the value of −VDC
2
+ 2 VDC
5
from fig. 4.3, which is equal to −400V , three upper
22
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:38==
submodules will be used, and two lower as well. The choice of submodules would be a
trivial decision assuming balanced capacitor voltages;
however,
it is not that
straightforward in real life. Capacitors are prone to imbalance, causing an increase in
circulating current [20] and, consequently, higher power losses. In order to keep the
capacitors balanced, it is necessary to know the voltage with which they are charged at
all times. The voltage of a submodule capacitor uC changes depending on the direction
of the current iupa as shown in fig. 4.5.
The principle for the balancing of capacitor
voltages is that:
• If the current flows with a positive sign, it will charge the capacitors. Capacitors with
the lowest voltage will be selected to charge.
• If the current flows with a negative sign, it will discharge the capacitors.
Thus,
capacitors with the highest voltage will be chosen to discharge.
Figure 4.5: Capacitor balancing (from [21]).
4.4
Generation of switching signals
So far, it is known how the submodules are selected to conduct based on the voltage
stored in their capacitors. Another relevant topic is to understand how switching signals
for the half-bridge submodules are computed. There are several methods available to
compute these signals, such as phase disposition-sinusoidal PWM, multilevel PWM and
Multilevel Space Vector Modulation (MSVM) [19], but in this thesis we will focus on the
Phase Shift Sinusoidal Pulse Width Modulation (PSSPWM).
The method described below is very similar to that in section 3.4.3, with the difference
that there are as many carrier waves as submodules. Assuming n submodules, allowing
for n + 1 voltage levels, n triangular carrier waves are required. In fig. 4.6, the modulating
waveforms for phase A vupma and vlowma from eq. (4.11) are compared to carrier waves
vcr−k, with k being the corresponding submodule. As a result, the switching signals supa−k
and slowa−k are generated for the upper and lower arms of phase A. Similarly, phase B
and C switching signals are computed.
An important factor about these carrier waves is that they have a delay of 2π
n with each
other, therefore depending on the number of submodules. Furthermore, they usually have
a switching frequency of j times the fundamental, this parameter being also limited by the
switching frequency of the electronic components. When these pulses are created, they
are arranged on the basis of the voltage of the submodule capacitors by the capacitor
balancing algorithm.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms

==PAGE:39==
Figure 4.6: Generated switching signals for the upper and lower arms (from [22]).
4.5
Control of MMCs
Now that the internal circuitry of an MMC is presented, it is time to discover how the
modulating waveforms va of eq. (4.11) for phase A, vb for phase B and vc for phase C are
generated. Similarly to what was explained in section 3.4.3, there are different control
modes for the MMC. They are all also based on two types of loop, outer and inner. The
inner current loop is very similar to that introduced for the wind turbine VSC. The shape
of the outer one is, however, conditioned by the operation mode of the MMC.
Grid-forming mode
The grid-forming or islanding mode is understood as the case in which a converter is
setting the AC voltage magnitude and angle of the AC voltage in the network to which it
is connected [23]. In short, the converter imposes the angle θ from a frequency setpoint,
which is later used in the ABC/DQ transformations. The converter plays the role of a slack
bus, providing a reference to the rest of the generators. The offshore grid frequency, in the
case of wind farms, is usually set to a fixed value, which is then enforced by the converter,
as seen in fig. 4.7.
foff

Figure 4.7: Voltage-controlled oscillator generating angle θ.
In order to generate the angle θ from the grid frequency foff, a Voltage-controlled
oscillator is used, which generates an output between -2π and 2π radians. This output
waveform is a ramp whose slope depends on the input foff. If the input is one, the
output will be a ramp whose slope is 2π/1. The output angle θ obviously depends on the
input frequency foff and is used to compute the DQ components of grid voltages vabc
and the currents iabc.

Grid-following mode
The grid-following mode is an alternative to the islanding mode in which the converter uses
an externally-imposed θ angle to decompose the currents into its DQ components. This
angle θ is normally obtained from the voltage of the network by means of a Phase-Locked
Loop (PLL) as shown in fig. 3.13. Thus, the fundamental frequency of the grid must be
known at all times. The outer control loops are used to set the active and reactive power
exchange or control the AC and DC voltages [23].
In the case of a MMC, the same outer control loops shown in fig. 3.14 can be used. For the
purpose of this thesis, the two additional control loops in fig. 4.9 are explained, employed
in the onshore MMC.
For the DC voltage control, the setup is very similar to that of the VSC but this time a
current droop is included. The aim is to keep the DC voltage setpoint within limits, even
for larger DC currents. Note that a saturation block ensures that the reference voltage Vdc
is kept within safe operating limits.
Regarding the AC control, it also includes a droop for the reactive power, increasing the
AC voltage reference for a larger reactive power flow. Note that, when computing the
error in AC voltage, the signs are inverted to those in fig. 3.14. This is a result of the sign
convention employed to measure the reactive power.
The inner, current loop is similar to the one described in fig. 3.16, taking two reference
currents [id∗, iq∗] and reducing their error by means of PI. Then, the cross-coupling terms
and the feedforward voltage terms are included to account for the coupling originated by
the MMC reactor. The outputs are the reference voltages [Vd∗, Vq∗].
In [24], several techniques to improve the DC voltage utilisation by the MMC are
discussed. For simplicity, the injection of the 3rd harmonic is considered in this thesis.
This creates a major difference when generating the modulating waveforms vabc = [va,
vb, vc], as the third harmonic contribution needs to be added.
The amplitude of this
component is controlled by gain h3, while the third harmonic angle is calculated from θ.
Note that this angle is three times larger than that of the fundamental harmonic. The

5
Analysis of short circuits
In electrical power systems, faults can be caused by insulation breakdowns, equipment
malfunctions, or external influences. These faults can be categorised into two types:
symmetrical faults and asymmetrical faults, depending on the number of phases that are
affected.
Symmetrical faults
Symmetrical faults, also known as balanced faults, occur when all three phases of a power
system have the same fault impedance and the fault currents are symmetrical with respect
to magnitude and phase. In such cases, the fault current flows uniformly in all three
phases and the fault is said to be symmetric. Although symmetrical faults are relatively
straightforward to analyse as they exhibit predictable and balanced behaviour, they are
the least frequent type. In fact, a frequency of only 4% of short circuit cases is often
assumed in the literature [25]. In fig. 5.1, a balanced fault in ABC phases to ground with
fault impedance Zf is shown. Note that the convention for fault impedance is consistent
with that in PF and PSCAD.
A
B
C
Three phase
Zf
Zf
Zf
Figure 5.1: Three phase, balanced fault.
Asymmetrical faults
Asymmetric faults, also known as unbalanced faults, occur when the fault impedance or
fault currents differ among the three phases. Since the current distribution among the
phases is no longer uniform, they are more complex and challenging to analyse
compared to symmetrical faults. The magnitude and phase angles of the fault currents
vary, leading to an unbalanced behaviour in the system. Figure 5.2 shows the three
types of unbalanced faults investigated in this project.
A
B
C
Zf
Single phase to ground
A
B
C
Two phase to ground
A
B
C
Zf
Phase to phase
Zf
Zf
Figure 5.2: Unbalanced faults.
Regarding the probability of faults, the single phase to ground accounts for more than
70% of the incidents, followed by two phase to ground with 17% and phase to phase
with 6% [25]. Note that these values are provided as a reference to illustrate that in
reality, unbalanced faults represent a large part of short circuits in electric power systems.
Therefore, a tool to simplify the analysis of these is critical.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
27
==PAGE:43==
5.1
The method of symmetrical components
This tool came as a method in the early 20th century, when the engineer Charles Legeyt
Fortescue demonstrated that, for a given collection of unbalanced phasors, these could
be expressed as the sum of a similar number of balanced phasors [26] [27]. In 1943,
Edith Clarke extended and simplified this method.
Assuming an unbalanced
three-phase system with ABC phase sequence ABC, she named positive sequence the
balanced set of phasors with sequence ABC, negative sequence to that with order ACB
and zero sequence to that set whose phasors are in phase with each other [28].
In fig. 5.3, the set of unbalanced phasors (Va, Vb, Vc) is represented by means of positive
sequence (Va+, Vb+, Vc+), negative sequence (Va−, Vb−, Vc−) and zero sequence
components (Va0, Vb0, Vc0).
In the literature, positive, negative, and zero sequence
components are commonly referred to with subscripts 1, 2 and 0, respectively.
Figure 5.3:
Representation of an unbalanced set of phasors by its symmetrical
components (from [29]).
Several books have been published covering power system analysis, such as [30],
which includes an exhaustive calculation of sequence networks for different types of
faults. Furthermore, [31] is more focused on the theory of symmetrical components and
is applied to the modelling of the power system in EMT. Lastly, [32] provides a more
intuitive approach to obtain sequence networks without requiring deep mathematical
insight.
5.2
The IEC 60909 standard
In order to simplify the analysis of fault events, there are standards that provide a
theoretical background to compute fault currents such as the IEC 60909 or the American
National Standards Institute (ANSI) C37.010. In [33], both standards are compared and
it is found that, in general, the first standard provides more conservative results for
equipment rating, mainly due to the introduction of a voltage factor c. However, this is
also claimed to not always hold true, as it depends mainly on the system X/R ratio. The
IEC 60909 standard has been shown to be widely used and is therefore the preferred
method for the scope of this project.
The aim of a complete short circuit analysis is to characterise the fault current during the
entire event. This is, however, not possible or not necessary in most cases, as the initial
symmetric short circuit RMS current and the peak short circuit current are enough to
dimension protection equipment [34].
The standard defines several parameters to
characterise the fault current waveform displayed in fig. 5.4 [34]:
28
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:44==
• ikss or i′′
k is the initial symmetrical short circuit RMS current
• ip is the peak short circuit current
• Ib is the symmetrical short circuit RMS breaking current
• ib is the peak short circuit breaking current, used in the Complete method in PF but
out of the scope of the IEC 60909 standard [35]
In addition, there are multiplication factors that help in the calculation of previous currents
[35]. These factors are varied depending on whether maximum or minimum fault currents
are computed [34]:
• c for the initial symmetrical short circuit RMS current
• k for the peak short circuit current
• µ is the symmetrical short circuit RMS breaking current
• m for the heat effect of the DC component
• n for the heat effect of the AC component
Figure 5.4: Fault current of a near-to-generator short circuit with decaying AC component
[35].
As can be seen, the fault current in fig. 5.4 is composed of an AC and a DC component.
The value of the initial peak short circuit current ip depends on the frequency and the time
constant of the decaying DC component, determined by the X/R ratio at the fault point.
In [36], it is concluded that the time constant τ of the DC component increases with a
larger ratio X/R, thus taking a longer time to decay. This decaying component can be
estimated by eq. (5.1), with ikss or i′′
k being the initial symmetric short circuit RMS current,
f the frequency, t the time, and R/X the inverse of the X/R ratio at the fault point.
iDC =
√
2iksse
−2πftR
X
(5.1)
The calculation method is based on the introduction of an equivalent voltage source with
value c · Un in the faulted bus, this being the only voltage source in the system. Un
represents the prefault voltage on the selected bus. In addition, the external grids and
machines are replaced by their internal impedance. Note that, while in most cases the
positive and negative sequence impedance will match, they can differ in power stations
with full-size converters [34]. This is a consequence of the current control employed in
the converters, as discussed in the previous chapter.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
29
==PAGE:45==
Given the scope of this thesis, the modelling of power station units with a full converter
is explained further. The design of the rest of the components such as grid transformers
and network feeders can be found in [34].
Power station units with a full converter
The IEC 60909 standard initially aimed to provide an estimation of the fault current for
classic power systems, with a high presence of synchronous machines.
However,
support for power systems with a higher penetration of power electronics has been
added in recent times. Note that even though the standard claims to be suitable for this
type of power systems, it still demands grid support from synchronous machines to a
certain extent. It is therefore interesting to assess how accurate the standard performs
in remote environments with a whole power electronics’ interface, such as the one found
in offshore wind farms with HVDC transmission.
In contrast to regular power station units, wind turbine station units can now be modelled
(1) as a single unit, including the machine and the transformer in the same component,
or (2) as an equivalent voltage source (following that approach for traditional machines).
The first was introduced in the last revision of the standard in 2016 and is thus the
preferred method. Power stations with a full-sized converter such as wind turbines or
photovoltaic systems, are modelled in the positive sequence as a current source [34].
The input parameters are the positive sequence current contribution and the negative
sequence impedance in the case of unbalanced faults [34].
The zero-sequence
impedance is considered infinite.
These parameters need to be defined inside the WT model [34]:
• Ik3PF is the RMS value of the maximum current contribution in three-phase faults
at the HV-side of the transformer in a power station unit with full size converter and
current regulation
• Ik2PF is the RMS value of the maximum current contribution in two-phase faults at
the HV-side of the transformer in a power station unit with full size converter and
current regulation
• Ik1PF is the RMS value of the maximum current contribution in single-phase faults
at the HV-side of the transformer in a power station unit with full size converter and
current regulation
• IkPFmax is the maximum steady-state current contribution in three-phase faults at
the HV-side of the transformer in a power station unit with full size converter and
current regulation
• IkPFmin is the maximum steady-state current contribution in three-phase faults at
the HV-side of the transformer in a power station unit with full size converter and
current regulation
• r2 is the negative sequence resistance
• x2 is the negative sequence reactance
Note that fault currents due to short circuits in the terminals of the generators are not
considered by the IEC 60909 standard [34].
Furthermore, the negative sequence
impedance Z2PF depends on the converter control mode.
30
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:46==
Part III
Methodology
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
31
==PAGE:47==
In the present part, a method based on the IEC 60909 standard for the obtainment of the
fault current during short circuit events on the offshore AC side of the wind farm is
proposed. Then, a verification of the method is conducted. First, standalone models of
the wind turbine and the HVDC link are created in PSCAD. Then, these individual
models are replicated in PF by means of a static generator model. Aiming for a similar
behaviour during faults in both software programmes, a comparison of the current
contribution during similar faults is made.
Once the standalone models have been
verified, they are aggregated into a wind farm model, both in PF and PSCAD. This
model is further validated by means of a steady state simulation. Finally, multiple faults
are placed at different locations of the wind farm and fault currents are recorded. By
comparing the fault currents from both programs, the error in per unit is found.
32
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:48==
6
Simplified method
In terms of the size of the protection equipment in the offshore wind farm, the fault
symmetrical current is often used as the key parameter. Estimations of the magnitude of
this current can be obtained, with more or less precision, by two types of software
programme. These two types of software correspond to the two kinds of phenomena in
power systems [37]:
• Electromechanical phenomena, such as those appearing in electrical machines.
These are typically simulated by means of a Root Mean Square RMS-based
software, in which all components are modelled for a fundamental frequency and
are represented by voltage and current phasors.
• Electromagnetic phenomena, as the one appearing in inductors and capacitors.
These are normally modelled in Electromagnetic Transients EMT-based software
and the full sinusoidal representation of voltage and current is considered. Thus,
instant voltage and current values are known at all times [38]
Each of these types has advantages and disadvantages, concluding that the decision for
one or the other depends on the user’s needs, given the programme capabilities. State-
of-the-art power electronics applications, such as the full-size converter of type-4 wind
turbines, require support for the simulation of high-frequency transient events. In addition,
the need for symmetric component analysis is explained by the fact that unbalanced faults
are the most frequent [38]. As a result of these two constraints, it is widely accepted to
use EMT-based alternatives for unbalanced fault analysis.
However, the use of EMT alternatives over RMS comes with a cost.
Although the
simulation time for fault analysis is normally restricted to a couple hundred milliseconds,
the full sinusoidal representation of voltage and current in EMT requires a much smaller
time step than RMS voltage and current phasors, leading to longer simulating time and
increasing computing power.
Furthermore, the degree of detail is generally larger in
EMT-based alternatives, allowing the study of transients with high frequencies but
requiring more complex models.
Furthermore, PF enables the simulation of power systems during fault conditions as a
steady state, thus being independent of time. This greatly reduces the modelling of the
system and is of great help in the system planning stage [35]. The software includes
different methods based on the IEC 60909 or the ANSI C37.010 standard, which are
independent of the pre-fault load flow. For a more specific analysis of short circuit currents,
PF offers the complete method, taking into account the pre-fault operating conditions of
the power system.
This thesis aims to develop a method to calculate the fault current during short circuit
events, minimising the simulation time and the model complexity. To compute the fault
currents in selected busses of the WF model, the IEC 60909 method in PF is used. This
is the preferred method because (1) it does not take into account the prefault conditions
of the system and, consequently, (2) requires less time for modelling.
6.1
The proposed method
The proposed process begins with the creation of a complete model of the wind turbine
and the HVDC link in PSCAD. These models are tested individually during faults to
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
33
==PAGE:49==
characterise their performance. Once this is achieved, a simplified twin model of these
components is created in PF.
Then, these models are tuned under the IEC 60909
standard to obtain current contributions similar to those obtained in PSCAD. At this
point, the fault current contribution of analogous models is compared and the magnitude
of the error is obtained. Once the standalone models are characterised, a wind farm
model is created in PF.
A load flow is computed to verify the desired steady-state
setpoints and equal cable parameters in both models. Finally, short circuit events are
placed in the desired locations and the fault currents are obtained.
Figure 6.1 shows the flow chart for the proposed method. The red steps refer to operations
in PF, while the blue ones refer to PSCAD. The purple colour is reserved for comparison
or common steps.

The IEC 60909 standard [34] suggests that two types of fault current must be calculated
from a system protection perspective. The first is the maximum short circuit current, which
is used to dimension switching devices and mitigate mechanical and thermal stress. The
second one is the minimum shot circuit current, employed to design the relays’ tripping.
As a result, both of these currents are included in the proposed method.
6.2
Verification of the method
To verify that this approach is valid, the results of the fault analysis for the same
locations and fault types in PF are compared with the results obtained in a detailed wind
farm model in PSCAD. Note that the PF method is entirely based on the IEC 60909
standard, which is not entirely intended for short circuit analysis in offshore wind power
plants based on HVDC transmission. Thus, the discussion of results will also provide
34
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:50==
insight into how suitable the standard might be for the calculation of short-circuit currents
in such environments.

7
PSCAD model
This chapter covers the process of obtaining the wind turbine and HVDC link standalone
models in PSCAD that provide the current contribution input to the component models
in PF. The components developed below take as a foundation the ones provided in the
PSCAD documentation [39], featuring (1) a type-4 wind turbine modelled as a PMSG and
(2) an HVDC link with an aggregated model of connected wind turbines. The fault current
contribution of each component was further investigated.
Once these two standalone models are developed, a setup of the entire wind farm is
created for verification purposes.
This model is used as a benchmark to assess the
precision of the method proposed in PF.
7.1
Wind turbine model
The model type-4 wind turbine model shown in fig. 7.1 was obtained from [39]. PSCAD
Documentation provides two components, ranging from a more detailed version of the
VSC-based converters including the IGBTs and Pulse Width Modulation to a VSC average
model employing voltage and current sources instead. This is motivated by the fact that
EMT simulations are time-consuming. Thus, the use of more lightweight, average models
may be suitable for some applications.
In the beginning, little knowledge was possessed about how the WT model behaved. To
investigate the capabilities of the VSC controllers during voltage dips and assess the need
for potential modifications, the initial fault test shown in appendix A.1 was performed. As
a result of this test, small changes were made to enable the operation of the wind turbine
along the MMC-based HVDC link.
The first variation is an increase in the medium voltage rating, from 33 kV to 66 kV, to
match current trends in offshore wind farms. During fault simulations in appendix A.1, it
was targeted that the 3-phase RMS voltage measurement was not representative
enough for unbalanced faults.
Therefore, the RMS positive sequence measurement
taken on the MV side was chosen as the voltage level indicator.
Additionally, other
minor cosmetic changes were made for display purposes. The resulting model is shown
in fig. 7.2. The specific parameters of the wind turbine model used in the simulations are
given in appendix B.1.
The PSCAD wind turbine model is made up of a series of submodules.
These are
explained in depth in the rest of the section, starting from the grid side of the wind
turbine towards the PMSG.
7.1.1
Aerodynamic model
The inputs to the wind turbine model are the wind speed and the number of wind turbines
that the model aggregates. The first parameter is fed to the aerodynamic model, shown
in fig. 7.3, which computes the active power available Pref_MW and the wind torque.
The parameters used for the aerodynamic model are shown in table B.2.
This aerodynamic model takes the wind speed, the current active power exchange
Pscale_MW, the current PMSG angular speed Omega_PM_pu and enable as inputs.
The active power exchange error and the current angular speed are used to calculate
the pitch required to extract the maximum power from the wind turbine. With the angular

7.1.2
Scaling component
The second parameter, the number of aggregated wind turbines nWT, is an input of
scaling component. This component emulates a larger wind turbine by means of the
Thevenin equivalent in fig. 7.4. The model uses Bergeron’s method to amplify the wind
turbine current by nWT similarly to a lossless transmission line of unitary length and a
delay of one time step [40]. The apparent power base, the voltage level and the per-unit
reactance are the input parameters shown in table B.3. Note that this component does
not exist in real life —it is merely used for simulation purposes.
Thus, this scaling
component reactance is subtracted from the MV/LV transformer real-life leakage
reactance. Therefore, the combination of the reactance in the scaling component and
that of the MV/LV transformer in the model is consistent with that of the MV/LV
transformer in real life. The parameters for this component are given in table B.3.
Figure 7.4: Scaling component of the WT. [40]
7.1.3
MV/LV transformer
In fig. 7.2, there is the medium-to-low-voltage transformer, which reduces the line-to-line
RMS voltage from 66 to 0.69 kV. The transformer is connected in a Wye-Delta
configuration, ground-isolated by a high resistor. Note that this configuration originates a
phase shift of 30°. As discussed above, the total leakage reactance of the transformer
must be considered as the sum of the scaling component reactance and the MV/LV
38
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:54==
transformer model leakage reactance to match real-life leakage reactance values. The
specific parameters for the transformer are shown in table B.4
7.1.4
Filters
Two filters (shown in fig. 7.5) are employed on the grid side of the converters to reduce
the influence of high-order harmonics generated by power electronics. This is done by
means of a cut-off filter that damps harmonics over 200 Hz. In [39] the value of Cfilter is
calculated with the inductance of the converter and the cutoff frequency. Then, a parallel
branch containing Ldamp, Rdamp and Cdamp is calculated to reduce oscillations due to
Cfilter and Lconv. The parameter configuration is shown in table B.5.

7.1.5
Grid-side converter controller
Both converter models take the same voltage references, which are generated in grid-side
and machine-side controllers shown in fig. 7.2. The theoretical background supporting the
control models of the VSC is explained in chapter 3.
The grid-side controller takes the voltage V breaker_kV and current measurement
Ibreaker_kA before the breaker as feedback signals, the positive sequence line-to-line
RMS voltage V ac_in_PU before the breaker as the indicator for the voltage dip and the
AC voltage reference, the DC link voltage V dc_link_V
and the reactive power
measurement Qscale_MV AR before the scaling component (in the connection point to
the offshore grid). This controller generates the reference voltage required to control the
voltages of the AC grid and DC link to the setpoints. The specific reference setpoints for
the grid-side controller are shown in table B.6.
Starting from the right, grid-side controller, it can be seen in fig. 7.6 that the first step is
the calculation of thetaPLL by the PLL component. This angle is obtained based on the
frequency of V breaker_kV , which the offshore MMC converter sets. Then, V breaker_kV
and Ibreaker_kA are decomposed into their DQ components using the angle thetaPLL.
These components are then filtered to reduce the influence of harmonics of a very high
order. Then, the current reference from the latter stage is subtracted from the converter’s
maximum current, which is 1.2 p.u. By doing this, the available margin for current is
computed based on the priority (in this case, the Q priority). Then, the VDC control is
found, where the error between V dc_link_V and the reference value of 1 p.u. is reduced
by the PI, generating a reference current D within the limits computed previously. Then,
the voltage V ac_in_PU is filtered, measuring if it is below 0.9 p.u. and, consequently,
detecting this as a voltage dip. A voltage dip will thus evolve into an extra Q current
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
39
==PAGE:55==
requirement —its magnitude depending on the voltage dip size. The error in the AC
voltage of the offshore grid is then controlled by the next PI. The output of this PI is
the reference to reactive power, and its error is computed by subtracting Qscale_MV AR.
This difference is then brought close to zero by the last PI, which generates the reference
current Q. Note that this Iq_ref_pu includes the additional Iq_ref_V oltage_dip current
that is added during voltage dips.
After the two current references have been generated in the outer loops, these are fed
to the inner current loop and compared to the DQ values that were decomposed in the
beginning. Then, two PI act on this error to reduce it. Finally, the inductance cross-terms
are subtracted from the output voltage reference to decouple the current control. In the
last step, the voltages are transformed from DQ parameters to a polar coordinate system
to limit the output voltage reference and protect the converter. Finally, this polar number
is transformed back into DQ components and ABC voltages. This V ref output is directly
fed to the grid converter.
4 - Vdc control
Obtain active Id_ref from DC voltage
2 - DQ decomposition
The grid-side controller controls the DC link voltage and the AC voltage in the MV side
6 - Vac control
Obtain Q reference from AC voltage in MV before the scaler
9 - Generate grid voltage reference:
The magnitude of the voltage in PU is multiplied by the LV, LN peak voltage to find the amplitude of the reference wave in kVpeak. Then, amplitude is limited to 1.15 kVpeak LN
5 - Voltage dip detection
Detect if the rms voltage in the MV side before the scaler is out of the normal range. If so, apply a gain to the rms voltage error to generate a Iq ref for Vdip

7.1.6
VSC converters
As mentioned above, PSCAD provides an average and detailed model of the wind turbine.
In [39], these two models are compared under dynamic conditions. First, the wind speed
is varied in the range [6, 15] m/s, leading to a decrease in active power generation as the
wind speed decreases. In the second case, a fault to ground in the Point of Connection
(POC) is tested, provoking a dip in the bus voltage. Under these conditions, both models
40
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:56==
have similar behaviour, but the total simulation time in the average model is reduced
seven times compared to the detailed one. This decrease in simulation time will become
crucial at a later stage, as the wind farm will be expanded with many more wind turbines.
Therefore, it was decided to use the average model as follows.
The latter model uses controlled voltage sources to generate the reference voltages
obtained from the controllers.
These voltages are then put into a 3-phase full wave
rectifier to convert the AC waveforms into a DC one.
Note that the current flow is
dependent on the voltage of the capacitor.
The inner parameters of both converters are shown in table B.7 and table B.10.

7.1.7
DC link and chopper
Inside the DC link, there is a chopper component, shown in fig. 7.8. This component is
enabled when the DC link voltage exceeds a defined threshold, typically around 1.15 p.u.
Once the DC voltage reaches that threshold, the resistor Rchopper branch is enabled and
power is dissipated until the voltage reaches the lower point of 1.05 p.u. The value of
Rchopper is calculated from the base power and the DC voltage. These parameters are
displayed in table B.8 and table B.9.
7.1.8
Machine-side converter controller
The machine-side controller takes the active power reference Pref_MW generated by
the aerodynamic model block and the active power measurement before the scaler
Pscale_MW as a feedback signal, the voltage V mac_kV and current measurement
Imac_kA on the machine side and the V ac_in_PU as a voltage dip measurement. This
controller generates the reference voltage required to control the machine voltage and
active power exchange on the DC link. The setpoints are shown in table B.11.
On the machine side, there is a control block that generates the reference output voltages
to control the active power exchange in the DC link and the AC voltage of the machine-
side network as shown in fig. 7.9. Initially, the machine-side AC voltage V mac_kV angle
thetaPLL is extracted by the PLL block. Using this angle, grid voltages and currents
are transformed to the DQ domain and filtered to eliminate higher-order harmonics. In

addition, the line-to-line positive sequence voltage V mac_PUrms is calculated and used
as a feedback signal for the magnitude of the AC voltage. Then, the reference currents’
margins are computed on the basis of the priority and maximum current, set to 1.2 p.u. Q
priority is used in the control as well. Moving on, the active power reference fed from the
aerodynamic model is compared against the one measured before the scaling component.
Note that this active power reference is multiplied by a gain that depends on the voltage dip
input signal V dip_in_PU. The reason behind this is to reduce the active power injection in
the DC link during faults. A PI reduces the error and generates Id_ref_pu. Next, the error
in the AC voltage is measured, and another controller generates the output Iq_ref_pu.
In the inner current control loops, the current error is measured and the inductive cross-
terms are subtracted to reduce the currents’ coupling. The output DQ voltage references
are then expressed in a polar system to limit the magnitude of the reference voltages.
Later, the value is transformed back to the DQ domain, and thus the output reference
voltages V ref are generated. These voltages are then fed to the machine-side converter.

7.1.9
Permanent magnet synchronous generator
Regarding the PMSG connected to the machine side of the converter, it has a nominal
power of 2 MVA operating at 0.69 kV line-to-line RMS. Furthermore, the rated frequency
is 30 Hz. Regarding the internal parameters, it has a stator winding resistance of 0.0017
p.u. and a stator leakage reactance of 0.0364 p.u. The angular moment of inertia is 4
seconds, as shown in table B.12.
Note that this machine is first controlled and initialised with an angular speed of 1 p.u. At
a certain moment, when all converters are already unlocked and their controllers are
working, the machine switches to torque control using the wind torque reference
generated by the aerodynamic model. Then, the machine starts extracting power from
the wind and the wind turbine injects active power into the offshore grid.
7.1.10
Fault tests
As part of the proposed method, an equivalent model of the wind turbine needs to be
created in PF. To characterise the behaviour of the wind turbine during faults, tests are
conducted on the MV and LV side of the WT component to obtain its current contribution.
These tests are explained in more detail in appendix A.1.2 and appendix A.1.3.
Table 7.1 and table 7.2 summarise the current contribution in kArms for different types of
faults on the MV bus (location F1), measured in the MV and LV side of the wind turbine
transformer. Columns A, B and C refer to the RMS value of the line currents, while I1,
I2 and I0 refer to the RMS sequence components. Note that these values have been
obtained as a RMS value from eq. (7.1), with t0=0.1 seconds, T=0.2 seconds and f(x)
being the input waveform shown in fig. A.7 and fig. A.8. Furthermore, the voltage retained
from the fault (p.u.) is shown in table 7.1.

7.2
HVDC model
The second standalone model, corresponding to the HVDC link model displayed in
fig. 7.10, was obtained as a template from the PSCAD Documentation [41]. The model
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
43
==PAGE:59==
features two Modular Multilevel Converters, their controllers, a high-voltage DC link and
an aggregated wind farm in the form of two generators.
Similarly to that of the WT model in PSCAD, initial fault tests were performed to
understand the behaviour of the HVDC link. During this process, it was discovered that
the aforementioned template did only include a current control in the grid-following
operating models. However, this was lacking for the desired grid-forming mode of the
offshore MMC.
Consequently, a positive-sequence current control was implemented
following the one shown in fig. 3.16.

Figure 7.11: Onshore MMC auxiliary.
Regarding the transformer, it has a power rating of 950 MVA. The primary winding is
connected in a wye configuration and has a rated line-to-line RMS voltage of 230 kV. The
second winding is connected in delta and has a rated voltage of 370 kV.
Then high-pass filter is found. This component takes the Q factor, the base and cutoff
frequency, the apparent power, and the phase voltage as input and computes the
equivalent RLC circuit.
The branch impedance decreases with increasing frequency.
44
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:60==
However, this branch is grounded through a large resistor, meaning that only harmonic
content of high order will be damped.
Figure 7.12 shows the onshore MMC controller, the component that generates the
voltage references required to reach the target AC and DC link voltages. The first step is
to obtain the voltage angle thetaPLL by PLL. This is the voltage angle on the side of the
transformer from the offshore grid. The current measurement is, however, taken on the
other side of the transformer. Given the Wye-Delta connection of the windings, there is a
phase shift of 30°that must be taken into account. This is done with the angle resolver
component, which generates the theta angle used for the decomposition of the DQ
currents. Then, the current margin is computed by subtracting the current references
from the maximum current constraint. Note that this current margin depends on which
priority is chosen —in this case, it is the Q priority. Moreover, the current is translated in
the polar domain to ensure that it is below the limits. If it is out of range, the voltage loop
PI are frozen.
The outer voltage loop is where the reference AC and DC voltages are subtracted from
the measured voltages to find the error, which is reduced by the PI. These PI generate
the Q and D current references, respectively.
The measured currents are then subtracted from the generated current references and
applied to other PI. Then, the p.u. voltages and cross terms are subtracted to decrease
the coupling and allow for independent control.
Note that the converter reactor
inductance is computed taking into account the converter reactor and arm inductances
as well as the transformer leakage reactance. Finally, the output reference DQ voltages
are transformed into the polar coordinate system to limit the voltage magnitude.
Likewise, the angle resulting from this transformation is subtracted from theta, obtaining
the third harmonic angle.
The reason why the third harmonic is considered is that it
allows a better use of the DC voltage [24].

3 - Outer Vdc control loop
6 - Generation of reference voltages
Decompose the reference DQ voltage to limit the magnitude to 1.5
Add the 3rd harmonic contribution to obtain the reference voltages for the converter

reactance

1 - PLL and DQ transformations
Note that theta is corrected due to the Y-Delta transformer phase shift of 30deg

7.2.3
MMC model
On both sides of the HVDC link, two MMC can be found. These are two instances of the
same component, which is displayed in fig. 7.13. This component features a Circulating
current suppression control model (in fig. 7.14) and three MMC PWM components
(shown in fig. 7.15), which model the upper and lower branches of each phase.
The specific parameters for the MMC onshore and offshore components are provided in
table B.15 and table B.18 respectively.

Figure 7.13: MMC component.
The Circulating current suppression control is a component that aims to decrease the
circulating current inside the MMC arms.
Circulating currents are generated by the
difference in phase voltages and lead to an overall higher RMS value, thus increasing
the power losses. Moreover, they appear in a negative-sequence form with a frequency
that is twice the fundamental (double-line frequency) [42].
The component takes the angle theta and the measured top and bottom arm currents.
First, the angle theta is used to compute the double-line angle, used for the DQ
decomposition.
Then, the top and bottom values of the current are averaged and a
negative sequence ACB-to-DQ transformation is computed using theta_2.
Note that
phase B and C are exchanged to follow the negative sequence rotating frame. Then,
these DQ components are expressed in per unit values.
So as to reduce the
negative-sequence DQ components, two PI are used. The two voltage references given
by PI are finally transformed back into the ACB domain. Note that theta_2 is again used
as the negative sequence rotating frame angle.
The voltage term generated by the CCSC control for each phase is used in MMC PWM to
compute the reference voltages for the upper and lower arms. Furthermore, the reference
voltage angle theta is used to generate 76 carrier waves, which matches the number of
cells on each arm of the converter. These carrier waves are then compared against the
reference voltages of the upper and lower arm, and a request for the insertion or bypassing
of each cell capacitor is found. A measurement of each cell capacitor voltage is taken
and the array containing the 76 cells is ordered to balance the capacitor voltages. The
requests, the arm current, and the ordered array are then put in the Firing Block. This
component generates the firing pulses for the top and bottom IGBT in each cell based on
the value of the arm current and the capacitor voltages.
46
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:62==

3 - Use PI controllers to minimize the error and cancel the DQ components
2 - Decompose the top and bottom phase currents into their negative sequence DQ components using

7.2.4
Chopper
There can be moments when the active power injection in the DC link is growing and the
HVDC link voltage starts to become too high as the DC voltage controller takes some time
to react and diminish it. In these cases, chopper detects these overvoltages and quickly
starts to dissipate energy, thus reducing the direct current in the cable and decreasing the
voltage. The specific parameters of the chopper are shown in table B.16.
The chopper component is shown in fig. 7.16. First, the DC link voltage is compared to
see if it has crossed the voltage limit that allows the pulse generator. This pulse generator
compares a triangular carrier wave against the computed deadband and generates a
pulse that is sent to the IGBT gate terminal and allows the current to flow through the
resistor. The size of the resistor is calculated from the input parameters in the 4th step.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
47

3 - Generate signal to turn on the IGBT by comparing the deadband against a 
triangular carrier signal

2 - Generate the deadband by comparing the measured DC voltage with the allowed one

7.2.6
Offshore auxiliary
Although the offshore auxiliary block is very similar in appearance to the onshore one, it
plays a different role, as the control sets the offshore AC voltage and frequency. This
component includes the offshore transformer, the converter offshore reactor and a
high-pass filter as shown in fig. 7.18. The specific simulation parameters are given in
table B.19.
Regarding converter reactor, it is modelled as a resistive-inductive branch in series with
voltage and current measurements. These measurements are taken on the converter
side and fed to MMC Controller.
