Figure 7.18: Offshore MMC auxiliary.
After the reactor, high-pass filter is found. This filter takes the Q factor, the base and
cutoff frequency, the apparent power, and the phase voltage as inputs and computes
the equivalent RLC circuit.
The branch impedance decreases with higher frequency.
However, this branch is grounded through a relatively large resistor, meaning that only a
high-order harmonic content (that with a frequency greater than fcut−off) will be
damped.
The high-to-medium-voltage transformer employs a Delta-Wye configuration, which is
relevant for fault studies as the delta connection damps the zero sequence current. This
is further explained in appendix A.2. Note that while the HVDC link power rating is 950
MVA, the transformer one is 300 MVA. This is chosen slightly over the wind farm branch
power output, which is 252 MW. In reality, three or four transformers of this type connected
after the converter filter would shape the wind farm.
The offshore MMC component is analogous to that onshore but differs in the controlled
parameters.
While the offshore controller could control the DC link voltage or active
power on the D axis and the AC voltage or reactive power exchange in the Q axis, the
offshore converter sets the offshore network frequency and AC voltage. This means that
the converter is operating in islanding mode, with an offshore frequency that is not
necessarily coupled with the onshore one. This mode of operation is common in weak
grids with a high penetration of renewables or remote areas isolated from the main,
stronger grid.
In the first step, the reference offshore frequency Freq_ref is fed to the Voltage
Controlled Oscillator, which creates an angle reference whose slope varies depending
on the frequency magnitude. This angle varies between −2π and 2π.
Then, this angle theta is used to decompose the voltage V1 and current Iabc measured
in fig. 7.18 in the DQ axes. The current margin is computed in a way similar to that of
fig. 7.9, taking the maximum current allowed as a constraint. Note that the current priority
is set to the D-axis.
The outer Vdq loop of this converter is different from the one implemented in the original
model. First, the reference voltage Q is null and the reference voltage D is normally set to
1 p.u. Two PI in this outer loop reduce the error and generate current references. Then,
the inductance of the reactor is taken into account to decouple the DQ currents. The inner
DQ current loop acts on the currents to minimise the error and generate the reference DQ
voltages. In the original model, DQ currents were not controlled and the output of the outer
DQ voltage loop was used directly as voltage references. The parts that were modified,
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms

Once the voltage references are obtained, the voltage is transformed into polar
components to limit its magnitude to 1.5 pu and transformed back into the ABC phase
voltages.

angle. The magnitude of this harmonic component is computed with the gain h3Mag,
and this harmonic contribution 3rd is finally subtracted from the output voltages. The
reason why this component is considered is that it allows a higher use of the DC voltage,
increasing from 1

1. Fault in the medium-voltage feeder.

7.3.1
Custom fault component
At each of these locations, a custom fault component is installed as shown in fig. 7.22b.
With the aim of reducing as much as possible the simulating time, the model was
automatised by (1) starting the simulation from two snapshots —steady state for
maximum and minimum current— and (2) creating two lookup tables reading external
files.
Two snapshots of this model were first created, one with the whole wind farm connected
and the second with only the HVDC link and converters on. The reason for having two of
these is to investigate not only the fault current value but also the contribution of each of
the components.
For the first look-up table, the input value (#Rank) is the current run of the simulation
and the output is the fault location, ranging from one to five. As for the second, it shares
the same input, and the output value is the fault type, taking the values in the comment
box in fig. 7.22a. By simply changing these external files, the model does not need to
be recompiled for each iteration. Furthermore, the fault resistance is chosen to be 0.1Ω
for the first four locations and 0.001Ωin the LV-side fault (Location 5), as decided in
appendix A.1.2.
There are five instances of custom fault component, and only one of them is activated in
each run when the value fault_location and the fault number match. In any other case,
the fault component is not active and behaves as an open circuit.
Various current and voltage measurements are placed inside this component to extract
the positive, negative and zero sequence RMS component of the fault current and the
52

7.3.2
Cables
Concerning the cables, three different models were used to simulate the different cross
sections used in the real setup. In fig. 7.20, the components for the three types of cables
are shown along with the length of each cable section. For example, the first cable Large
2000 in string A has a length of 2000 metres and is the type of cable with the largest
ampacity. In fig. 7.23, a view of the inner π-equivalent model of this cable is presented.

7.3.3
Steady-state and dynamic tests
Once the wind farm layout was assembled, four tests were performed to assess the
proper performance of the system.
The first one covers the start-up sequence until
steady-state values, while the remaining three dynamic tests simulate the system
response under variations in frequency, wind speed, and voltage.
As a result, it is
concluded in appendix A.3 that the system has great flexibility in terms of operation for
varying frequency, wind speed, and voltage conditions.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
53
==PAGE:69==
8
PF model overview
This chapter presents the model used for the fault calculation in PF. First, two standalone
models for the offshore MMC and the wind turbine are created with the aim of simulating
the maximum current injection during faults of the analogous PSCAD models.
Once these models are fully tested and verified against those in PSCAD, they are
aggregated to a wind farm model, employing the same layout, components and cable
cross sections as the one in PSCAD.
8.1
Wind turbine model
Regarding the wind turbine, it was modelled in PF as the static generator shown in fig. 8.1.
This model has a unity-rated power factor and does not include a transformer inside the
component. The generator control mode is set to constant voltage in the LV bus of 1
p.u. Note that the generator is rated for 12 MVA and is connected to the 0.69 kV LV
bus. Thus, the scaling block present in the analogous PSCAD model is not needed, but
its impedance is, however, considered inside the LV/MV transformer. This transformer
is rated for 12 MVA and has a positive leakage reactance of 0.125 p.u. in the given
base. The resistive losses equal to 0.001 p.u. and the negative sequence impedance is
assumed to be the same as the positive one. The transformer is connected in a Wye-Delta
configuration, corresponding to a YNd11 scheme in PF. In addition, the High voltage (HV)
side is grounded through a resistor of 1 MΩ. All these parameters are equivalent to those
of the PSCAD model in appendix B.1.

Figure 8.1: Model used for the characterisation of the static generator
The component of the voltage source in fig. 8.1 provides a reference grid to the wind
turbine, as it operates in grid-following mode and therefore cannot set the frequency of
the grid by itself. This component is modelled with a target voltage of 66 kV at the MV
bus. Tests were also conducted to characterise the voltage source sequence impedance
54
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:70==
in PSCAD. As a result, it was found that the voltage source has the same positive, negative
and zero sequence impedance with r012 = 50.02Ωand x012 = 157.08Ω
The IEC 60909 standard recommends the use of a voltage factor c between 0.95 and 1.1
for the calculation of maximum and minimum short circuit currents [34]. With this tuning
method, this is set to unity instead, as it was not possible to differentiate these two cases
when obtaining the input values from the PSCAD standalone model.
In chapter 5, the IEC 60909 standard support is introduced for wind power units with
full-size converters. These are represented by means of a current source behind an
impedance in the positive sequence network [34]. Given this representation, the initial
symmetrical short circuit current contributions I′′
k for each type of fault need to be
specified. Since one phase to ground, two phase to ground, three phase to ground and
phase to phase faults are investigated, four I′′
k must be specified.
Note that these
currents are referred to the HV side of the transformer. The corresponding values for
initial currents I′′
k are taken from the positive sequence current column in table 7.1.
This representation also forces the zero sequence impedance to be infinite and the
negative sequence an input parameter. The latter depends on the type of current control
implemented in the wind turbine [34].
In the case of the wind turbine developed in
PSCAD, the converter control employs a positive sequence control, which limits the
current within the operating range. The negative and zero sequence components are,
however, not limited by a current control. Therefore, it is assumed that the negative
impedance will have a finite magnitude.
Because of the inductive behaviour of the
converter reactor, the magnitude of the impedance will also depend on the frequency.
There is extensive literature proposing methods for the calculation of this impedance,
but the complexity of such methods discourages their use given the scope of the thesis.
Alternatively, the negative sequence impedance of the wind turbine is found by manual
adjustment of each fault. First, the positive sequence symmetrical short-circuit current
contribution I′′
k for the actual current fault type. The negative sequence reactance x2
is established in 0.1 p.u. and an initial negative sequence resistance r2 of 2.5 p.u. is
assumed for the first iteration. Then, a short circuit analysis is performed with a fault
on the MV bus and the negative sequence current in the MV side is compared to the
value obtained in PSCAD. The resistance r2 is then increased or reduced accordingly to
increase or reduce the negative sequence current, and this is repeated until the error is
below 0.0001 kA. Then, the process is carried out for the rest of the faults. Finally, a string
is obtained containing all negative sequence resistances r2.
Using columns I1 and I2 of table 7.1 as positive and negative sequence inputs for the
tuning process, the simulation parameters in table 8.1 are obtained.
Table 8.1: Positive and negative sequence current and negative sequence impedance of
the equivalent static generator model.

8.2
HVDC link model
Now that the equivalent model for the wind turbine has been obtained, it is time to
discuss the implementation of the HVDC link equivalent. From previous sections, it is
known that offshore MMC operates in grid-forming mode, providing an AC voltage and
frequency reference. Consequently, the HVDC including the offshore MMC is modelled
as an External grid in PF and also operates as the slack bus for load flow calculations.
This model is shown in fig. 8.3. Note that the RLC filter is not considered for short-circuit
current contribution but is, however, displayed to match the wind farm model overview.
RLC
External ..
Harmonic ..
MV Bus
HV Bus
Figure 8.3: Model used for the characterisation of the external grid
56
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:72==
In IEC 60909, a method is given to calculate the positive sequence short circuit
impedance for three-phase symmetrical faults, with the initial symmetrical short circuit
current I′′
kQ as the input parameter.
In addition, it states that the zero sequence
impedance may be taken into account depending on the start-point connection and
grounding [34].
An initial test was performed with the positive sequence impedance
computed after this method, but the current contribution during unbalanced faults
deviated considerably from the PSCAD results. Thus, a second method is proposed,
which is based on the aforementioned manual tuning. Note that this method assumes
the voltage factor c from the IEC60909 standard to be unity. Otherwise, higher fault
currents than those in PSCAD can be expected due to the larger equivalent voltage
source.
The method starts by adding the short circuit power SCC, which can initially be assumed
to be the full apparent rated power of the HVDC link. Then, the currents obtained from the
PSCAD test in the MV bus are specified and used as a reference. Note that the tuning is
done on the MV current contribution directly, as faults on the HV side of the transformer
will not be studied. Then, the values in fig. 8.4 are used as initial parameters for the r1/x1
ratio, the z2/z1 ratio, and the x0/x1 ratio. A first short circuit simulation is performed and
the r1/x1 ratio is adjusted accordingly based on the positive sequence current error. If the
short circuit power SCC is not large enough to provide the required current, this parameter
is increased, and the process is restarted.
As soon as the error in the positive sequence current is below 0.0001 kA, the parameter
r1/x1 is saved, and the tuning of z2/z1 starts. There, it is initially set to 50 and then
increased or decreased until the error is within the specified margin. Note that if more
than 25 iterations are performed, the model is considered not to converge. In such a
case, SCC increases and the tuning of r1/x1 and z2/z1 is repeated. A similar process
is followed for the adjustment of x0/x1, which is performed in the last place. When all
three components are found, the algorithm iterates over the next fault type. As a result,
three different arrays containing the r1/x1 ratio, the z2/z1 ratio, and the x0/x1 ratio are
obtained. Their length matches the number of fault types.
As a result of the tuning process, the following parameters were obtained:
Table 8.2: Parameters obtained of the tuning process for the HVDC model in PF.

As seen in chapter 6, a maximum and minimum current must be calculated. In the case
of the External grid component in PF, it allows the independent configuration of the
parameters in table 8.2 for the two cases mentioned above. However, it is not possible
to differentiate between maximum and minimum short circuit currents using the
standalone model in PSCAD. If the same values in table 7.1, are used for the two
current adjustments, similar configuration parameters are obtained.
As a result, the
parameters in table 8.2 are used to calculate the maximum and minimum short circuit
current. In addition, the voltage factor c is chosen to be unity for the same reasons as
were mentioned above.

8.3
Wind farm model
Once the standalone models are created and their parameters tuned, it is time to configure
the rest of the wind farm model. Figure 8.5 shows the model used for the simulations in
PF. In the top left corner, the External Grid component is found, which models the HVDC
link and the MMC. This component controls the voltage at the local HV bus to be 1 p.u,
similar to the MMC VAC control in PSCAD.
Further down, the converter reactor can be found. This has a series inductance of 0.25
mH and 0.5 mΩ, mimicking the values of table B.19. In addition, a harmonic filter is

added with a power per phase of 10 MVAr, a resonant frequency of 2400 Hz and a quality

9
Verification of models
This chapter presents the verification of the models developed by comparing the results
of PSCAD and PF. This verification is first performed for the standalone wind turbine and
HVDC link models and later for the wind farm layout.
9.1
Wind turbine model
In previous chapters, the current contribution of the wind turbine model to a fault in the MV
bus was presented. Subsequently, the negative sequence impedances of the analogous
standalone model were tuned to obtain a similar current contribution in PF.
The resulting line currents on the MV side of the transformer can be compared with those
obtained in PSCAD (shown in table 7.1) in table 9.1. The error is computed as the absolute
error divided by the rated base current, thus being given in p.u. For the medium voltage
side of the transformer, this base current is computed as follows:

By comparing PSCAD and PF results in table 9.6, the absolute relative error related to
the PSCAD measurement for active power, reactive power and voltage can be found.
In the case of active power, the highest relative error is found at the beginning of string
C, equating to 0.1%. This is a very low value that verifies that the model behaves as it
should in terms of active power.
Concerning the reactive power, it can be seen that the maximum error is 9.1% for the
case of the measurement taken on the converter side of the transformer. The fact that
this is the largest error is consistent, as the error in the strings propagates. It is also seen
that the closer the wind turbine to the MV bus, the larger this error is. These errors may
be a consequence of the lack of the AC filters and converter reactor of the wind turbines
in the PF model.
Regarding the voltage, it can be seen that the highest error is found in the wind turbine
B1, although this error of 0.6% is almost negligible.
Given all the previous results, it is assumed that the PF model is verified against the
PSCAD one, assuming that there will be some small reactive power differences due to
the simplifications taken in PF.

10
Fault testing
This chapter addresses how the fault current measurements are obtained in PSCAD and
PF. Following on the fault cases and locations defined before, twenty simulations need
to be performed in the case of the maximum fault current, covering five fault locations
(F1, F2, F3, F4 and F5) and four types of faults (single phase to ground, two phase to
ground, three phase to ground and phase-to-phase faults). In the case of the minimum
fault current, only 16 cases are simulated, since the fault F5 in the LV-side is skipped due
to the disconnection of the WT. These cases are shown in fig. 10.1.

Figure 10.1: The set of simulations conducted in PSCAD and PF.
10.1
PSCAD
In PSCAD, the simulations have been configured to run in parallel to minimise the
simulation time. In addition, this time can be further reduced if a snapshot is created,
covering the wind farm initialisation. The fault event takes place at second 4.9, lasting
200 miliseconds. During this time, voltage and current in selected locations is measured
and recorded. An example of the output waveform is shown in fig. D.5.
In that plot, it can be seen that the fault current waveforms differ significantly from those
defined in the IEC 60909 standard and shown in fig. 5.4.
By analysing the plots in
appendix D, it was concluded that the current waveforms did not present an initial peak
as the one infig. 5.4.
Instead, the symmetrical AC component of the fault current
seemed to increase slightly towards the fault event end. This is mainly caused by the
fact that the MMC and wind turbine converters limit the output current, as shown in
fig. 3.15.
To compare against the standard, an analogous current measurement to ikss was defined.
Thus, the instant output values for current and voltage displayed in fig. 3.15 are imported
into MatLAB, where the RMS function defined in eq. (7.1) is applied in the range [0.1, 0.2]
seconds. As a result, the RMS fault current ikss is obtained for each of the phases, as
well as the symmetrical components and the retained fault voltage as shown in fig. 10.2.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms

==PAGE:79==

Figure 10.2: Obtainment of the ikss equivalent in PSCAD.
10.2
PF
In PF, voltmeters and ammeters do not need to be placed to obtain voltage and current
measurements. Furthermore, PF provides greater support and integration to Python than
PSCAD, allowing the use of scripts to export and conduct simulations in sequence. For
the scope of the thesis, a script was developed to extract the short circuit simulation results
as an Excel file. For the current, results are obtained in kArms and, for the voltage, in p.u.
values.
Concerning the short-circuit calculation options, PF provides the option to use the voltage
factor c defined by the IEC 60909 standard or one defined by the user. This voltage factor
is applied to the equivalent voltage source on the faulted bus, increasing or decreasing the
prefault voltage as described in chapter 5. The selection of the voltage factor depends on
the voltage level of the faulted bus and whether the maximum or minimum fault current is
to be obtained. As a consequence, the results will be more conservative for larger factors
c. For the scope of the thesis, and given the assumptions taken for the HVDC link tuning,
the values provided in the standard are not used and a unity c factor is used instead.
10.3
Calculation of the error in p.u.
Once both PSCAD and PF have voltage and current measurements, it is time to compare
the current and voltage measurements. For the currents, this is done by computing the
absolute error between both measurements and then dividing by the base current in each
part of the wind farm. These base currents are calculated as shown in eq. (9.1) and
eq. (9.2), obtaining a LV-WT base current of 10.041 kArms, a MV-WT base current of
0.105 kArms, and an MV-MMC base current of 8.310 kArms. These values are used as
follows:
• Ib−RMS=10.041 kArms for the LV fault current error (fault F5 only).
• Ib−RMS=0.105 kArms for all WT contributions errors.
• Ib−RMS=8.310 kArms for all MV fault current errors (faults F1, F2, F3, and F4), MMC
contribution and string current measurements.
In the case of the voltage, the error is given also in p.u. but it is not divided by the base
voltage, as the output of both PSCAD and PF is already in p.u. values.
64
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:80==
Part IV
Results
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
65
==PAGE:81==
The following part presents the results of the proposed method, applied to fault analysis
in offshore wind farms based on HVDC transmission. First, a model is implemented in
PSCAD and is taken as a benchmark against which the second model is evaluated.
Second, the PF model is generated following the method introduced in part Part III,
which applies the IEC 60909 standard to the fault calculation. Third, the error in fault
current and voltage measurements between both models is calculated and the error in
the fault current contribution from selected components is also measured, both for
maximum and minimum fault currents, as discussed in the previous chapter. Finally,
these results are discussed to analyse how accurate the proposed method is compared
to the one developed in the EMT-based software.
The legend in the following figures is explained as follows:
• Ph. A, Ph. B, Ph. C: phase A, B and C fault current.
• I1, I2, I0: positive, negative and zero sequence components of the fault current.
• Error median: median on the fault current error, calculated from [Ph. A, Ph. B, Ph.
C, I1, I2, I0].
• V1, V2, V0: positive, negative and zero sequence components of the retained
voltage at the faulted bus.
• ConA, ConB, ConC: phase A, B and C current contribution of the MMC.
• strA_A, strA_B, strA_C: phase A, B and C current contribution of the string A.
• strB_A, strB_B, strB_C: phase A, B and C current contribution of the string B.
• strC_A, strC_B, strC_C: phase A, B and C current contribution of the string C.
• A1A, A1B, A1C: phase A, B and C wind turbine A1 current contribution.
• A7A, A7B, A7C: phase A, B and C wind turbine A7 current contribution.
• B1A, B1B, B1C: phase A, B and C wind turbine B1 current contribution.
• B7A, B7B, B7C: phase A, B and C wind turbine B7 current contribution.
• C1A, C1B, C1C: phase A, B and C wind turbine C1 current contribution.
• C4A, C4B, C4C: phase A, B and C wind turbine C4 current contribution.
The error median is introduced to provide a visual reference of the overall error, as there
are several errors for each fault type and location. Additionally, each radial axis represents
a location:
• F1: fault in the medium-voltage feeder.
• F2: fault in the first wind turbine A1 of string A.
• F3: fault in the farthest wind turbine A7 of string A.
• F4: fault in the farthest wind turbine B7 of the longest string (B).
• F5: fault in the low-voltage side of the furthest wind turbine (B7).
Furthermore, the type of fault is indicated as follows:
• A-gnd: fault from phase A to ground.
• BC-gnd: fault in phases B and C to ground.
66
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:82==
• ABC-gnd: fault in all three phases to ground.
• BC: fault between phases B and C.
In the following figures, all axes belonging to the same radial plot use the same scale.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
67
==PAGE:83==
11
Maximum fault current
In this chapter, the results for the calculation of the maximum fault current using the
proposed method are presented. The condition for a maximum short circuit current is
that all system components are configured so that they deliver the maximum current
contribution. The following graphs show the error in current and voltage measurements
between PSCAD and PF for the maximum fault current, expressed in p.u. values.
11.1
Medium voltage faults
As described in part Part III, five fault locations are studied —four of them are in the MV-
side of the wind farm. In the following figures, the current and voltage error at the faulted
bus, as well as the error in current contribution from selected measurements, is displayed
by means of a radial plot. Note that all measurements were taken on the MV side of the
wind farm and are referred to as the corresponding base current.
Figure 11.1: Fault current error [p.u.] at the faulted bus for a maximum short circuit current
(Ib−RMS=8.310 kArms).
The absolute highest error in fig. 11.1 is given for the ABC-gnd fault in F1. This error
amounts to 0.311 p.u.
Because it is an ABC-gnd fault, negative and zero sequence
networks are shorted to ground [44]. Since negative and zero sequence circuits do not
68
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:84==
Figure 11.2: Retained voltage error [p.u.] at the faulted bus for a maximum short circuit
current (Vb−RMS=66 kVrms).
have a voltage source, there is no I2, I0 current flow [44]. With respect to the positive
sequence current, the network is grounded through the fault impedance. This holds true
for the PF results, but in PSCAD there is a residual negative sequence current flow due
to pre-fault voltage imbalance. However, this residual current does not explain such a
high error by itself. In fig. D.10, it can be seen that the fault current of 11.296 kArms
matches completely the scalar sum of the contribution from the strings and the MMC.
On the contrary, this does not happen in PSCAD. Consequently, it is suspected that fault
currents are assumed to have the same phase angle in PF for the ABC-gnd fault in F1,
although this requires further investigation.
For symmetrical faults, the current injection of the MMC exceeds that of the strings by
approximately one order of magnitude (as in in fig. D.10). In light of these results, it can
be claimed that the MMC is the main contribution in terms of the fault current.
Taking a look at fig. 11.4, one can see that the A-gnd fault also has a relevant error
in phase A of 0.275 p.u. Smaller error is found for the current sequence components.
In this fault, sequence networks are connected in series [44]. Thus, the same current
I1 = I2 = I0 flows through the fault impedance, which increases by a factor of three [44].
In general, this error is introduced mainly by the MMC (ConA). In addition, the error in the
current contribution of the wind turbines is higher than for other types of faults.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
69
==PAGE:85==
Figure 11.3: MMC and strings contribution error [p.u.] for a maximum short circuit current
(Ib−RMS=8.310 kArms).
A considerably smaller error is found for BC and BC-gnd faults. The phase fault current
error is in all cases below 0.16 p.u. Likewise, the MMC phase B error (ConB) is around
0.17 p.u. for all locations of type BC and smaller for type BC-gnd.
The gray line in fig. 11.1 connects the median faults computed from the phase and
sequence current error for each type and location of fault. It can be seen that this value
is maximum for the ABC-gnd fault at location F1 and below 0.1 p.u. for the rest of cases.
In PSCAD, the WT model detected the voltage dip as soon as the positive sequence
RMS voltage in the local MV bus was below 0.9 p.u. Consequently, Low Voltage Ride
Through (LVRT) was activated and the active power reference was reduced, leading to
a decrease in the injection of active current and an increase in the injection of reactive
power. However, this mechanism is not implemented in PF. Thus, the current injected
during the fault is merely determined by the path impedance and the initial current ikss for
each case, but does not take into account current controls. This is evident for the ABC-
gnd fault in F3, where the current contribution of wind turbine B7 obtained in PF (0.124
p.u.) stays relatively constant while the one from PSCAD is substantially reduced (0.077
p.u.).
Regarding the retained voltage during the fault, a maximum error in the positive sequence
voltage of nearly 0.12 p.u. is obtained for the BC-gnd fault at location F4. In addition, a
70
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:86==
Figure 11.4: Wind turbines contribution error [p.u.] for a maximum short circuit current
(Ib−RMS=0.105 kArms).
smaller error of 0.11 p.u. is found for an A-gnd fault in location F4. In fig. 11.2, it can be
seen that the error in the voltage measurement is generally lower than that of the current.
11.2
Low voltage faults
One special case of the maximum fault current is the calculation of the short circuit current
for the low voltage fault F5. For clarity purposes, these results have been kept separate
from the rest of the faults. The following figures show the error in the fault current and
voltage measured at the WT LV-side and the contribution from the MMC and the strings
measured on the medium voltage side.
Concerning the fault current during LV fault events in F5, the maximum error is given for
the A-gnd fault, which amounts to 0.81 p.u. In fig. D.10, it is shown that the fault current
from PF for this case is null. The reasons for this are that (1) sequence networks are linked
together in a series [44] and (2) the zero sequence impedance of the wind power unit under
the IEC 60909 standard is infinite [34]. Consequently, sequence currents have the same
value and are null, since they flow through an infinitely large impedance. Additionally, the
transformer shown in fig. 7.21 is connected in a Delta/Wye configuration, which blocks
the zero sequence current on the MV side of the wind turbine. It is fair to mention that the
standard discourages its use for the calculation of faults on the low-voltage side of wind
power stations, which is confirmed given the results obtained.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
71
==PAGE:87==
Figure 11.5: Fault current error [p.u.] at location F5 for the maximum short circuit current
given a LV fault (Ib−RMS=10.041 kArms).
For BC-gnd faults in PSCAD, all three networks are connected in parallel [44], so a zero
sequence current flow can be expected. However, this zero sequence current is null due
to the infinite zero sequence current of the static generator in PF. This fact induces a
higher error. Additionally, the rest of the wind farm cannot contribute with zero sequence
current given the delta connection in the WT transformer.
Concerning the ABC-gnd fault, one would expect that the error would be lower as the
negative- and zero-sequence networks are disconnected. However, it can be seen in
fig. 11.5 that the largest median of error is found for this type of fault.
The BC fault has the positive and negative sequence networks connected in parallel, while
the zero sequence network is not linked [44]. Hence, the flow of current through the zero
sequence impedance is avoided, and results closer to those from PSCAD are found.
As for the retained voltage error, the maximum value is 1.48 p.u. and the rest of errors
are considerably high.
Taking these values, it is clear why the IEC 60909 standard
discourages its own use for the calculation of the fault current on the LV-side of wind
turbines. However, it can be also seen in fig. 11.7 that the error when it comes to the
MMC and strings current contribution is below 0.1 p.u. in most cases, so it could still be
useful to assess the current contribution of such devices to low-voltage short circuits.

Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:88==
Figure 11.6: Retained voltage error [p.u.] at location F5 for the maximum short circuit
current given a LV fault (Vb−RMS=0.69 kVrms).

for the maximum short circuit

74
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:90==
12
Minimum fault current
Concerning the minimum fault current, the IEC 60909 standard mentions that the
contribution of wind power units should be neglected [34]. In PSCAD, this translates into
keeping the WT circuit breaker open during the whole simulation, while in PF all wind
turbine models are de-energised. As a result, the fault on the LV side is not considered
as the wind turbine is disconnected and the current contribution of all wind turbines is
null. The following figures show the minimum fault current and voltage error in p.u. for
faults in locations F1 to F4.
Figure 12.1: Fault current error [p.u.] at the faulted bus for the minimum short circuit
current (Ib−RMS=8.310 kArms).
As mentioned in chapter 10, there are several assumptions that are taken when
concerning the minimum fault current calculation. One of them is that the HVDC link
contributing current is presumed to be the same as in the maximum short circuit current
event. As a result, the voltage factor c is kept in unity for the calculation of short circuit
currents in PF.
Concerning the minimum fault current, a maximum error of 0.294 is found for the BC-gnd
fault at location F2. This is explained by the fact that PF fault current positive and zero
sequence components are smaller than those obtained in PSCAD. Note that the negative
sequence current is considerably smaller than the positive and zero sequence, explained
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
75
==PAGE:91==
Figure 12.2: Retained voltage error [p.u.] at the faulted bus for the minimum short circuit
current (Vb−RMS=66 kVrms).
by the higher negative sequence impedance of the MMC for BC-gnd faults (from table 8.2).
In addition, it can be seen that ConC and strA_C errors are also around 0.29 p.u. For this
fault, the contribution of the MMC and the string A measurement is lower in PF than in that
of PSCAD. In fig. 12.1, BC-gnd faults have the second largest median error after ABC-gnd
faults.
For that type, the median error is maximum at location F3. In this case, the fault current
error in phase C amounts to 0.293 p.u. This contrasts strikingly with the errors of BC and
A-gnd faults —where the maximum error of all cases is below 0.1 p.u.— and to lesser
extent with BC-gnd faults. It is also remarkably higher than the error at the rest of locations
(F1, F2 and F4) due to the lower MMC current contribution from PF.
In the calculation of the maximum currents, it was stated that the method accuracy relied
mainly on a correct configuration of the model simulating the HVDC link. In the case
of minimum currents, this is even more crucial and is demonstrated by the fact that the
points in fig. 12.1 match approximately the location of those in fig. 12.3. This proves that
the injection of MMC current will determine to a larger extent the fault current error than
that of the WT.
Concerning the voltage error, it can be observed in fig. 12.2 that it is below 0.2 p.u. in all
cases. For the ABC-gnd, the error in voltage is almost null. A very small value of the fault
impedance leads to a voltage dip of almost 0.98 p.u.
76
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:92==
Figure 12.3: MMC and strings contribution error [p.u.] for the minimum short circuit current
(Ib−RMS=8.310 kArms).
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
77
==PAGE:93==
13
Discussion of results
Taking
all
the
previous
points
into
account,
some
general
conclusions
and
recommendations on future work can be extracted.
• The fault current and retained voltage error for the LV fault is considerably higher
than for the MV faults. These results suggest that the IEC 60909 standard is not
suitable for the calculation of fault currents and retained voltage in low voltage
faults if WT are modelled as wind power stations with a unit transformer.
Alternative modelling methods within the IEC 60909 standard should be explored
to verify if they allow a higher accuracy.
• In contrast to that, a smaller error is obtained for the MMC and wind turbine
contribution to the fault current for low-voltage faults than for medium-voltage
faults. This suggests that the method can be used for estimations of such a current
contribution.
• The fault current estimation in the MV-side presents an error below 0.3 p.u. for all
the cases in the maximum and minimum fault current cases.
– For the maximum current, the largest error is found for an ABC-gnd fault at F1
and the median error is around 0.1 p.u. for the rest of the cases.
– For the minimum current, the largest are faults ABC-gnd at F3 and BC-gnd
at F2. The median error is bigger for the ABC-gnd and BC-gnd faults than in
A-gnd and BC faults.
• The tuning method for the MMC and WT models currently only takes into account
the fault current magnitude, but not the phase. The possibility of using models in
PF that allow for fine tuning of sequence impedances is to be explored. This could
also further reduce the retained voltage error.
Naturally, this would impose the
requirement for extra information about the sequence components’ phase from
PSCAD.
• In both the maximum and minimum short circuit currents, the MMC is the component
that contributes the most in terms of fault current. Therefore, a correct validation of
the analogous model in PF is critical to obtain an acceptable fault current error.
• Part of the MMC error is introduced by the assumption that its current contribution
will be the same for maximum and minimum fault currents. A better tuning could be
performed by obtaining the current in more remote faults.
• Similarly, the fact that the current control of the converters is not taken into account
in the proposed method is a source of error for some of the faults, where a higher
ikss than that in PSCAD is obtained. These current controls could be implemented
in PF using the RMS package described in part Part III.
• Finally, the present results are valid for the chosen current control. Should the
converters control the current in any other fashion (for example, with negative
sequence current control), the results would differ from those presented before.
78
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:94==
Part V
Conclusion
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
79
==PAGE:95==
In conclusion, this master’s thesis aimed to address the challenges associated with
analysing short circuit faults in HVDC-based offshore wind farms. A simplified method
was proposed and implemented to calculate short circuit currents based on the IEC
60909 standard in PF, a power system analysis software. The accuracy and reliability of
this simplified method were compared with those of a detailed electromagnetic transient
(EMT) model implemented in PSCAD.
First, initial tests were conducted in PSCAD to characterise the behaviour of the wind
turbine and the MMC under different fault events. Then, the standalone models in PF were
tuned following the proposed method to achieve similar results. The individual models
were then added to a simplified wind farm. Finally, a steady-state simulation was carried
out to verify that active and reactive power flow throughout the wind farm were consistent
in both models.
By measuring current and voltage at different points of the circuit, the fault current and
the retained voltage can be obtained both in PF and PSCAD. In addition, the current
injection from selected components is also studied to analyse whether deviations in the
fault current have these as an origin. Finally, results are obtained by comparing the error
between the measurements of both programmes.
The results obtained from the comparative analysis of short circuit currents between PF
and PSCAD provided valuable information on the suitability of the IEC 60909 standard
for fault analysis in offshore wind farms based on HVDC transmission. The proposed
method demonstrated reasonable accuracy in estimating fault currents and retained
voltages at the medium voltage side of the wind farm, while supporting the statement
that the same standard should not be used for faults on the low voltage side of the wind
turbines.
However, certain limitations were also identified.
First, that the proposed
method accuracy relies to a large degree on the correct tuning of the MMC model, as it
is the main contributor to the fault current (surpassing in many cases the current
contribution from wind farm strings by approximately one order of magnitude). Second,
the proposed tuning method takes only into account the sequence current magnitude,
but not the phase. A more advanced method should also deal with the adjustment of the
phase angle, potentially reducing the retained voltage error. Naturally, this requires to
extract further information from the detailed EMT models.
Future research can focus on addressing the identified limitations of the proposed method,
such as studying how converter current control can be implemented in PF and refining the
modelling and configuration of the MMC. Furthermore, exploring other available models
for the wind turbine, included in the IEC 60909 standard, could provide a behaviour closer
to reality in terms of accuracy. In particular, models that allow for a higher customisation
of the sequence impedance for both the MMC and wind turbines are of interest.
By providing a simplified method for short circuit analysis and evaluating its accuracy
against a detailed EMT model, this research contributes to the field of electrical
engineering and assists in the development of effective protection strategies for offshore
wind farms based on HVDC transmission.
80
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms

Furthermore, it explains the tests that were carried out in

Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
85
==PAGE:101==
A
Characterising the components in
PSCAD
Before creating the entire wind farm model in PSCAD by merging the developed wind
turbine and HVDC link models, the models were individually tested with specific types
of faults to assess (1) the relationship between voltage dip and fault impedance, (2) the
performance of these components during faults, and (3) the current contribution of the
standalone models. Once the PSCAD wind farm model was obtained, further tests were
conducted for steady-state conditions as well as by varying the references for frequency,
wind speed and voltage.
A.1
Wind turbine tests
This section covers the simulation of a wind turbine individually under different voltage
dips and the contribution in current for faults in the low voltage and medium voltage side
of the wind turbine.
A.1.1
Influence of the fault impedance
Four different types of fault are simulated (1-phase to ground, 2-phase to ground, 2-phase
and 3-phase to ground) for different voltage dips (0, 0.3, 0.5, and 0.7 p.u.) and duration
(0.15, 0.3, 0.5 and 1s respectively). The fault location is in the Point of Connection bus
displayed in fig. 7.1.
A first iteration is performed for each type of fault with a different fault impedance to identify
which delivers the desired voltage dip. Given the asymmetrical nature of some of the
faults, the minimum voltage is determined not only by the fault impedance, but also by the
fault type. In practise, it translates into three of the four types not reaching the retained
voltage of 0 p.u. This is explained by the fact that there is a voltage remaining in some of
the phases. The minimum voltage level during faults is shown in fig. A.1.

Positive-sequence
Negative-sequence
Zero-sequence
Figure A.1: Fault voltage levels of A-ground, AB, AB-ground and ABC-ground faults for
low fault resistance during varying fault duration.
As it can be seen in fault AB in fig. A.1, the reference active power of the PM machine is
not reduced during the fault. This indicates that the voltage dip is being measured poorly,
and therefore it is not being detected by the controllers. The voltage dip measurement is
the 3-phase line-to-line RMS voltage:
VRMS−Eq = 1
3 · (V 2
AB,RMS + V 2
BC,RMS + V 2
CA,RMS)
(A.1)
For symmetrical faults, VRMS−Eq is similar to the single-line RMS voltage during
symmetrical faults. However, during asymmetrical faults, one of the phases may present
a lower single-line RMS voltage than the other. In some cases, the average VRMS−Eq
will be lower than some of the single-line RMS voltage, but in others it will not be low
enough to detect a voltage dip. As it is desired to have a common indicator, the voltage
dip is then defined as the magnitude of the positive sequence RMS voltage.
Figure A.2 shows a comparison between the original voltage dip detector and the modified
one. In the first figure, this voltage boundary is set to 0.8 p.u. and the 3-phase line-
to-line RMS value is used instead. Therefore, the voltage dip control is not activated.
As expected, the second figure shows a decreasing active power reference in the PM
machine when the voltage dip control is activated, which happens as soon as the positive
sequence voltage falls below 0.9 p.u.

Fault
Figure A.3: Location of the fault inside the wind turbine.
As can be seen in appendix A.1.2 and appendix A.1.2, the higher fault resistance in
appendix A.1.2 forces the retained voltage to remain around 0.3 p.u., while a smaller
fault resistance allows more current to flow, thus obtaining a lower retained voltage.
However, this fault current does not differ significantly in both cases, as the RMS value
of the positive sequence component of the current is steady around 10.5 kA. What is
actually changing is the stability of this waveform, as it oscillates more when the fault
resistance is decreased.

appendix A.1.2.

imbalance that increases the voltage magnitude of the healthy phases while sinking the
unhealthy one. However, no significant deviations appear between the retained voltages

Figure A.5: A-to-ground fault currents in fig. A.3 for Rfault = 0.01Ωand Rfault = 0.001Ω.
With regard to current, it is worth mentioning that, for single phase-to-ground faults, the
positive, negative, and zero sequence components are in agreement and take the same
value as seen in appendix A.1.2. More interesting might be that due to the Wye-Delta
90
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:106==
connection of the transformer in fig. A.3, the zero sequence current cannot flow from the
MV side to the LV side. This current can flow from one winding to the other, but it stays
trapped in the delta loop and does not contribute to the line current.
Taking into account all the above, a fault resistance of Rfault = 0.001Ωwas chosen for the
fault location 5 on the LV side of the wind turbine as it allows for a lower retained voltage.
A.1.3
Fault on the MV side
In order to study the current contribution of a fault on the MV bus, the setup in fig. A.6
was employed. Note that the external grid is modelled as a voltage source behind an
impedance, with X/R ratio of 3.14 corresponding to an RL series impedance of 50+157.07j
Ω.
MV_Bus
A1
I_WT
1
Fault
RRL
Figure A.6: Test setup for the HVDC fault in the MV bus.
As for the fault, it has a fault impedance of 0.1 Ω, leading to a very large voltage dip. It is
also known that this voltage dip will be maximum for the 3-phase-to-ground type of fault.
In fig. A.7 and fig. A.8, the current contribution is measured in the MV and LV side of the
transformer inside the wind turbine. Note that the measurement for the MV-side of the
current of the WT transformer is not taken within the fault block, but in I_WT in fig. A.6. If
it had been taken in the fault current, the results would include the contribution from the
external grid.

A.2
HVDC link tests
Similarly, the same faults are replicated in the high-voltage DC link and MMC model,
placing the fault at the offshore grid end, as shown in fig. A.9. First, the fault impedance is
varied to check its influence on the voltage dip. Then, the fault resistance is kept constant,
92
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:108==
and the current contribution of the offshore MMC during faults is studied.
A.2.1
Influence of the fault impedance
Four types of fault are simulated for different fault impedance —1-phase to ground (in
fig. A.11), 2-phase to ground (in fig. A.12), 2-phase (in fig. A.13) and 3-phase to ground
(in fig. A.14)— all of them occurring in the 2nd second and lasting half a second.
Figure A.9: The HVDC link model with the Modular Multilevel Converters and the block
simulating the faults.
Inside the component MMC Auxiliary we can find the MMC controller, the converter
reactor, the filter, and the connected transformer. Note the two different connections in
the transformer: wye in the offshore grid side and delta in the converter side.
Figure A.10: The MMC control and transformer.
The following values were used for the simulation:
Table A.1: Simulation parameters.

Regarding the single-phase-to-ground fault that can be seen in fig. A.11, the minimum
voltage dip is limited to approximately 0.7 p.u., which matches the result shown in
fig. A.1. Moreover, it can be seen that the phase imbalance in the fault originates with
the appearance of a negative sequence and a small zero sequence voltage.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
93
==PAGE:109==
Taking the current measurements on both sides of the transformer, on the grid side there
is a certain zero sequence current flowing that is not present on the offshore converter
side. This is explained by the fact that the converter side of the transformer is connected
in a delta configuration, thus having no neutral return path (shown in fig. A.10). Note
that the delta configuration reduces the contribution of the current third harmonic current,
which is present and flowing inside the delta loop but is not propagated to the line current.
Furthermore, the wye configuration enables lower insulating requirements on the grid
side but requires a neutral path. Therefore, this neutral path on the grid side allows the
zero sequence component of the current to flow, while lack on the converter side of the
transformer blocks it.

contribution of the zero sequence to the current, reaching almost the same value as the

in wye configuration. Consequently, instantaneous current measurements on both sides
of the transformer deliver different readings, especially in the case of phase C.

Concerning the phase-to-phase fault in fig. A.13 it can be seen that while the zero
sequence does not contribute due to the lack of a grounded path, the negative and

converter side the negative sequence of the current is slightly smaller than the positive
one.

measurements in different phases, particularly different for phase C.

Figure A.13: 2ph fault for different voltage dips.
Regarding the three-phase to ground faults, it can be seen in fig. A.14 that for asymmetric
faults, the negative and zero components of both voltage and current remain small. Note
that it is with this type of fault with the one that the largest voltage dip is found, which
explains why the literature refers to symmetric faults as the most severe. Even with such
a large voltage dip, the control system is capable of restoring the offshore grid voltage to
the target 1 p.u. setpoint within 1 second.

Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:112==
A.3
Wind farm tests
When the two standalone models for the HVDC link and the wind turbine were modeled,
they were aggregated into the wind farm model.
Later, this model was tested under
steady-state conditions for varying frequency, wind speed and voltage setpoints.
A.3.1
Start-up sequence
Regarding the start-up sequence, the different components of the system are enabled in
this order:
• At 0.2 seconds, unlock the onshore MMC converter. The DC link voltage measured
onshore increases approximately from 0.8 to 1 p.u., as seen in fig. A.18.
• At 0.6 seconds, unlock the offshore MMC, which starts to set the offshore AC grid
to the reference 1 p.u. and sets the system frequency to 50 Hz. Further, the DC link
measurement suddenly falls and returns to the reference (1 p.u.).
• At 1 second, the breaker that isolates the equivalent wind turbine from the offshore
AC system is closed.
• At 1.1 seconds, the WT grid-side converter is enabled, controlling both the offshore
grid AC voltage and the DC link voltage. This instantly raises the voltage of the WT
DC link from the initial 0.95 to the 1 p.u. setpoint.
• At 1.2 seconds, the WT machine-side converter is enabled, injecting active power
in the DC link. This creates an overvoltage in the wind turbine DC link and, as
a result, the chopper acts dissipatng energy. After some instants, the grid-side
converter reacts to the overvoltage extracting power from the DC link and injecting
it in the offshore grid. The AC voltage of the offshore network also increases for
some instants, but returns to the target 1 p.u. rapidly.
• At 1.7 seconds, the permanent magnet machine switches its control mode from
speed to torque control. This torque reference is obtained from the aerodynamic
control of the wind turbine.
• At 1.9 seconds, the active power injected by the machine in the WT DC link error is
reduced and the active power reaches its final setpoint of 1 p.u.
• At 4 seconds, the reactive power consumption of the wind farm stabilises. Therefore,
the start-up sequence is assumed to be completed.
It can be seen in fig. A.18 that there are some significant oscillations in active power
measurement in the onshore network, mainly due to the connection of both HVDC
converters. Moreover, the wind turbine DC link experiences overvoltages as the WT
machine-side converter starts injecting active power into the DC link. This overvoltage
triggers the chopper, which is activated when the DC voltage reaches 1.15 p.u. and
deactivated when it is below 1.05 p.u.
These oscillations disappear as the grid-side
converter detects the DC link overvoltage and thus starts extracting active power from
the DC link.
To avoid having these deviations during start-up transients, steady-state conditions are
taken as the starting point of fault studies. On a practical level, this means that a snapshot
will be created at 5th second, and all fault simulations start from those conditions.
A.3.2

In fig. A.19, the offshore reference frequency is varied between 49 and 51 Hz from the
fourth second on. It can be seen that, although there are some fluctuations due to the

A.3.3
Change in offshore AC voltage reference
In fig. A.20, the wind farm response is displayed in response to a change in the setpoint
of the offshore AC voltage. In this simulation, the offshore AC voltage passed as an
input to the offshore MMC is varied between 0.9 and 1.1 p.u. As can be seen, the positive
sequence RMS voltage, measured on the MV bus where the first fault is placed in fig. 7.20,
follows the AC voltage reference although it does not fully reach the 0.9 p.u. reference.
This is suspected to be a consequence of the current limitation in the offshore MMC to
protect its internal electronics, even though the maximum AC voltage limits are not met.
In addition, reactive power is injected into the offshore grid during the increase in voltage
reference.
When this AC voltage reference increases to 1.1 p.u., the reactive power flow in the

Additionally, changes in the offshore AC voltage reference have an impact on both the
HVDC and the DC voltage of the WT link, more moderate in the first. In the latter, some
instantaneous oscillations appear reaching 1.15 p.u.
Figure A.20 shows that the wind farm is capable of operating at different offshore AC
setpoints. Furthermore, it proves that changes in offshore AC voltage mainly affect the
reactive power flow direction in the HVDC link.
A.3.4
Change in wind speed
The third and last test that was carried out was that in which the wind speed fluctuated
between 2 and 26 m/s. Initially, all wind turbines were producing at rated active power,
close to 1 p.u. or 12 MW. Then the wind speed is reduced from 15 to 2 m/s. It can be seen
from the active power export of the wind turbine that the active power starts to decrease
after eq. (3.3) within the aerodynamic model of the wind turbine and, once the cut-in speed
of 3 m/s is exceeded, the export of active power is cancelled. This provokes a decrease
in both the DC voltages and the onshore AC voltage.
In 5th second, the wind speed starts to increase again and the aerodynamic model
generates a reference to non-null power. This increase in the exported active power
boosts the DC and AC voltages until controllers react and these voltages return to
nominal 1 p.u. When the wind speed exceeds the cut-off velocity of 25 m / s, the wind
turbine is stopped and the power output is reduced to zero. A decrease appears in the
AC and DC voltages.
If this wind speed is now reduced to the initial 15 m/s, the power reference created by
the aerodynamic model sets the converters to export active power through both DC links,
which generates a momentary overvoltage in the AC and DC measurements.
Figure A.21 confirms the wind speed as the input parameter that affects the most power
extraction. Furthermore, it has been proven that wind turbines can adapt the pitch to
optimise power extraction at different wind speeds and that the wind farm can operate for
different wind gust conditions. Moreover, sudden decreases in active power are seen to
provoke a decrease in AC and DC voltages, and the other way around.

Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
==PAGE:122==
D
Assessing the accuracy of the method
In this appendix, the simulation results from PSCAD and PF for different faults and
locations. First, a summary of all faults is offered, followed by the plots containing the
fault currents and bus voltages. For faults at locations one, two, three, and four, a fault
resistance of 0.1Ωwas used, while for the fifth location, 0.001Ωwas employed.
In
addition, a comparison of the contribution to the fault current by the offshore MMC, the
strings and wind turbines A1, A7, B1, B7, C1 and C4 for the same type of fault in
different locations is provided. Finally, the error in p.u. is computed.
D.1
PSCAD
The first part of the appendix provides the waveforms obtained in PSCAD for each type of
fault and location, from which the ikss value was obtained by applying the RMS function
in the range [0.1, 0.2] seconds.

D.2
Error
This section covers the contribution of the offshore MMC, the strings and the wind turbines
A1, A7, B1, B7, C1 and C4 to the fault current. Later, the error in p.u. between PSCAD
and PF measurements is provided.

Figure D.11: Fault current and voltage, contribution from selected components and error for the minimum fault current case.
Faults transient analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms
119
==PAGE:135==
The integration of wind energy into the power grid is of utmost importance in meeting the growing
demand for renewable energy sources. High Voltage Direct Current (HVDC) transmission systems
have emerged as a promising solution for offshore wind farms, offering advantages over traditional
AC transmission systems. However, analysing transient events for protection of such systems,
particularly short circuit faults, poses challenges in terms of simulation complexity. This master’s
thesis aims to address these challenges by proposing a simplified method for calculating short
circuit currents in PF, based on the IEC 60909 standard, and comparing its accuracy against a
detailed Electromagnetic Transient (EMT) model implemented in PSCAD.
The study commences with an extensive review of literature encompassing wind turbines, HVDC
transmission systems, mathematical tools employed for fault analysis as well as a description of
the IEC 60909 standard fundamentals. Subsequently, a comprehensive standalone model of the
wind turbines and the HVDC transmission system is developed in PSCAD and their behaviour
during faults is characterised. Later, two analogous models are obtained in PF and a comparative
assessment under steady state conditions is conducted to evaluate the accuracy and reliability of
the PF standalone equivalent against the detailed EMT model in PSCAD.
Upon validating the standalone models, two symmetrical models of an HVDC-based offshore wind
farm are constructed in both PF and PSCAD. Multiple fault scenarios are simulated at various
locations within the wind farm, capturing the contributions of selected components to the fault
currents in both software programs.
The outcomes of this thesis encompass the comparative analysis of short circuit currents between
the IEC 60909-based model in PF and the one in PSCAD, contributing to the field of electrical
engineering by providing a simplified method for short circuit analysis and ultimately assessing the
suitability of the IEC 60909 for fault analysis in HVDC-based offshore wind farms.

University of
Denmark
Frederiksborgvej 399
DK-4000 Roskilde
